Ernest Faveno’s juvenile story, “ The Secret of
the Australian Desert,” will be published about
next Xmas as a half-crown volume (posted, 2s.
XOd.), with four illustrations by Percy Spence. It
describes the adventures of three white boys and
one black in the mysterious bowels of our con-
tinent ; and is liberally sprinkled with wonders
and blood. The bill of contents, including canni-
bals, a burning mountain, and traces of lost
Leichhardt, is very appetising.

literature (which didn’t sell a-quarter as well), has
been proved at .£BO,OOO.

A REAL CHINAMAN.
Passenger T. C. Crane, floating on his
meat-safe after the wreck of the
was addressed by a Chinaman who swam up
to partake of the meat-safe’s hospitality.
“ More better you go catchey ’nother piecey
box,” said the Chinaman. “ More better
you,” responded Mr. Crane, winking a
watery eye ; and the colloquy ended.
That pregnant conversation was conducted
in “ pidgin-English,” the vernacular of the
Chinese treaty-ports. “Pidgin-English” is
a corruption of “ business English,” and
Chester Holcombe, in “The Real China-
man ” (cloth, Bs.) —the most interesting book
ever written about China—gives other amus-
ing examples of its use.
When King Kalakaua, of the Hawaiian Islands,
was in Shanghai in April, 1881, he occupied a
suite of rooms up one flight of stairs at the Astor
House. Two American gentlemen, desiring to
pay their respects to His Majesty, went to the
hotel one morning, and meeting the proprietor at
the foot of the stairs, made known their errand,
and enquired if the king was in. “I will see,’’ re-
plied the landlord, and tu lining on his heel, he
shouted to a Chinese servant at the head of the
stairs; “Boy! That piecey king top side hab
got?” “Hab got,” laconically responded the
servant. “ Gentlemen,” said the landlord, “His
Majesty is in. Pray walk up. ”
With such quaint gems do Mr. Holcombe’s
pages sparkle. His style is a model—
nervous, compact, restrained, the embodi-
ment of supple strength. Generally in
repose, it sometimes leaps out on you with a
fine image—as in a contrast of Japan with
China :
Japan, in discarding Oriental fashions for Wes-
tern, was, in the main, merely casting off borrowed
clothing. Those of China, on the contrary, are of
her own devising, and have been slowly and
laboriously woven as the shuttle of time passed
to and fro through many centuries.
Even did space permit, no quotation could
do “The Real Chinaman” full justice. Every
page has its plum; and for each it is possible
to pick hundreds remain on the tree. The
book deals with Chinese government, lan-
guage, home and social life, religions—there
are three commonly practised ; Confucianism,
Buddhism, and Taoism superstitions,
queues, courts of law, officials and people,
education and literature, etiquette and cere-
mony, merchants and tiicks of trade, the
poor, and the Chinese financial system. It
has the vital quality of complete knowledge
—so far as complete knowledge of China is
possible to a European ; the author was for
10 years secretary to the U.S. Legation at
Peking, and, an able observer, he seems never
to have missed one of his exceptional oppor-
tunities for observing.
One abridged extract, to illustrate the
methods of Chinese justice. A number of
Chinese are charged with assaulting -an
American :
The magistrate, a venerable and pleasant-faced
man of 70, turned to one of them and said, in a
familiar -way :
“You tell us all about it now. Why did you beat
the American ? Tell us the whole story.”
“ But I did not beat him, your Excellency,”
said the man. “ I wasn’t there. Besides, lam a
good man, A thousand times ten thousand it is
true I never touched him.”
“Oh, yes ! we know all about that,” rejoined
the magistrate. “ Such a good son of Han as you
ure wouldn’t harm a baby ; but, all the same,
what made you do it ? Of course, we know you
did not do it; in fact, you were in bed and asleep
when the deed was done. At the same time, it
will save us a lot of bother if you confess. Of
course, you did not mean any harm ; we are sure
of that. You wanted a little fun, and had heard
some talk about the streets that if someone wmuld
only frighten the foreigner, or give him a drub-
bing, he would leave town and never return,’and
so you thought you would try it. 'Certainly you
were not there ; but you beat him just the same
as though you were.”
Thus the garrulous old man ran on for more
than an hour, quizzing, bantering, and persuading
the accused, who, whenever an opening gave him
chance to be heard, reiterated his innocence, but
in tones noticeably growing fainter and less posi-
tive. At last he said : “ But I did not hit him as
hard as some of the others did.” Quick as a flash
the magistrate retorted : “You did hit him, then?
Come, now, tell us about it.” And then the
prisoner, with his head hanging down and with
the air of a school-boy caught sticking pins into
the master’s chair, said: “Well, if I must, I
must. Perhaps 1 did give him a shove.”
“ That is right,” said the judge, “ we knew you
did ; but it is always better to confess.”
Eight shillings is a good deal for a book in
these times ; but, to the seeing mind, this
book is well worth it.
PERIODICALS.
Public Opinion is an English paper (20s. 6d.
per annum posted throughout Australasia) which
has not nearly the circulation it'deserves. Its
method has been copied by the Review op
Reviews, but Public Opinion is much the more
valuable. In the first place, it is weekly and up-
to-date, instead of monthly and behind-hand.
Then it scissors its stuff with more discrimination
to secure current interest, and good articles are
clipped in extenso, instead of being lopped to an
irritating Procrustean measure. To the far-away
person with a shallow purse and a hankering to be
kept abreast of topics of the day, Public Opinion
is strongly recommended as the cheapest and best
compendium available.
“ Form at a Glance ” (Is. Id.), is worth every
racing-man’s money. It indexes admirably the
performances of every horse entered for Caulfield
and Melb. Cups, 1895; and tabulates in addition
the weight-for-age scale; fastest time records;
past winners; length of principal race-courses ;
registered colours, and current weights.
“Sarony’s Sketch-Book” (Is. Bd.), of which
nine numbers are now issued, has had a deservedly
large sale in Australasia. Some of the early num-
bers contained real gems of art in the way of
photos, from the nude, and the level is still fairly
maintained. Each number contains 12 pictures,
often reproductions of celebrated paintings ; and
the publication, as a whole, is one of the most
artistic ever issued. The only points for cavil
are the insignificant features of the models
who represent Cleopatra, Zenobia, &c. The forms
are generally all right, though some of them err
considerably on the side of dumpiness; but the
faces are strikingly characterless and expression-
less.
The Englishwoman (12s. a year; single numbers,
9d.) maintains a good level as a ladies’ magazine.
Marie Belloc’s articles on “French Authors of
To-day ” are a pleasant feature. The English-
woman is more modem than the Woman at Home
(single numbers, 9d.; per annum, 125.), which
is respectable and a little heavy.
The first three numbers of the “ Penny Poets ”
series—Macaulay’s “Lays,” Scott’s “Marmion,”
Byron’s “ Childe Harold, ”&c., (first two cantos) —
are well worth a 2d. stamp each.

As a rule, to print a book in London costs about
half as much as to print it in Australia. This
fact naturally has weight with even patriotic
authors who bring out their own books —especially
as unpatriotic readers add a cwt. or so by showing
marked preference for a London imprint. But cir-
cumstances seem changing. Francis Kenna’s charm-
ing poems were recently issued in a very neat
edition of 250 copies, which cost about A 25 to
produce, or 2s. a copy. The London price
would have been about <£l7 10s., but the author
would probably have lost the advantage of correct-
ing his proofs—how great an advantage only those
who have suffered from erratic English printers’-
readers can testify! Mr. J. D. Hennessey’s
clever new Australian novel, “ The Dis
Honourable ” (35.), which covers between 200'
and 300 pages of long-primer type, was printed
and bound in Sydney at a cost of about <£loo for
20t;0 copies, including a complete set of stereo,
plates. This is about the cheapest Australian rate
on record.
lan Maclaren, whose “Bonnie Brier Bush ” a
leading Sydney bookseller (Scottish) praises as the
best of all recent samples of kailyard literature,
preaches, as the Rev. John Watson, at Sefton
Park church, Liverpool. His new volume, “ The
Days of Auld Lang Syne, ” is in the press.
Paperknife : “ Everything’s in a name. Mrs.
Humphrey Ward, for “The Story of Bessie
Costrell,” annexed over .£lOOO from her publishers.
The story is worth about 20s. ”

We wish It to be understood that the character of the
business we do, and the absolutely inclusive variety of the
goods we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are also not far out, considering the low prices we
ask for everything we sell, and the consequent blessings
we are continually showering upon the people, when we
assert, not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourselves pIJBLIO BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life —honest
citizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stands
out in bold relief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the few
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little band
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who, in self-abnegation and contentedness, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but if the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surely the laborer is worthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
never cease from their toil ; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they work, oppressed by the cruel frost of
somewhiie baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
it has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.
ANTHONY HORDERN & SONS,
UNIVERSAL PUOVIIMEKS.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MICK O’ROURKE, EVANGELIST.
It was Mick O’Rourke, the Ringer, bailed up
Father Flynn one day,
As his Reverence was riding by upon his ewe-
necked bay—
Mick was shearing then at Tarbuo, in the neigh-
borhood of Hay.
“ Is it Michael ? ” said the clergyman ; who knew
him as a droll
Sort of sinner ; tough as Satan ; yet a white man,
on the whole;
“ Son, I trust that you are doing well, and think-
ing of your soul ! ”
“ Thank your Rivirence,” said Michael, “ ye wor
always mighty kyind ;
lam doin’ purty middlin’; and me sowl is well-
inclined :
But the rayson why I stopped you is—l’m
throubled in me mind !
“Since we started here, at Tarbuo, I’ve been
Ringer of the shed—
Though I’ve had to fight, as well as shear, to keep
a bit ahead —
Faix, I put the fear of Fisht in thim that had no
other dhread.
“And I’ve done my best to keep the shed in
ordher from the first,
Though there’s chaps here that I think wor in
religion’s lap d/iry-nursed
There’s a cove or two from Pinthridge, and a man
from Dariin’hurst.
“ But they’re dacent min to work wid —though
there’s some av thim have odd
Ways wid kyards, and talkin’ slang, sor, which
they larnt, no doubt, in quod—
Barrin’ wan, a Milburrne chap, who says he don’t
believe in God.
“Sor, a more conthrairy customer than him I niver
saw;
There is no one here can aiqual him at workin’
wid the jaw :
Which comes aisy to him, seein’ he was brought
up to the law.
“Well, I’ve raysoned wid him calmly, and me
words in meekness dhrist;
I’ve remonsthered wid a shovel, and I’ve argeyed
wid me fist ;
But I’ve failed to make a Ohrischin av that Mil-
burrne Atheist.
“ So I thought, when I tuk notice av yer Rivirince
passin’ near,
I would ax ye what was best to do, and ye wud
make it clear;
For I mean to give the shed a sign—“ No Hay-
thens wanted here. ”
“ Mick, ray son,” said Father Flynn, “ don’t try
to bear another’s load !
Let God’s judgment fall upon him—as it will in
some strange mode.”
And the priest gave Mick his blessing, and con-
tinued on his road.
Mick the Ringer smiled : “ Troth, Father Flynn,
I’ll take yer hint,” said he;
“If God’s judgment’s sure to fall on him —why
shouldn’t that mane me?
Faix, I’ll fall upon him, suddint-like —’twill save
his sowl, maybe.”
There’s a maimed and broken vessel, with an ear
that’s gone astray,
That goes preaching round the stations from
Deniliquin to Hay ;
If you ask him for his story this is what he’s sure
to say :
“ I was once an Unbeliever —yea, a man of Sin
and Shame,
Till at Tarlcuc, ’midst affliction dire, converted I

CREEVE ROE.
RECONCILIATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Brown, burnt-armed, he worked from sunrise
until sunset day by day,
Axeing, clearing, burning, fencing, building,
laying out a home,
And when done he caught his horse and, canter-
ing the nearest way
Townward, found his wife, law-sundered years
ago, and bade her come.
“Nan,” he simply said, “come with me ! ” Nan
looked up in mute surprise
And beheld long, wild night-revels, splashed
with blood and drenched with tears,
And the sword of legal sev’rance; but behind
these saw the eyes,
And within the eyes the heart that throbbed to
hers in far-off years.
And her answer was, “I will.” Then at the
words Love, with a cry,
Seized and killed the wild night mem’ries, and
the sword of sev’rance broke ;
And above the re-united spread a re-illumined
sky,
And the old songs of the dead years once again
to music woke.

strengthen the Silken Bonds, uphold the glorious
flag, &c., and liberally subsidised by the Aus-
tralians, has found its proper level. It is now,
confessedly, a mere market for the London demi-
monde, and was lately described in the Commons
as “a cross between Cremorne Gardens as they
were, and Burlington Arcade as it is.”

By sending direct to Tim Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—

Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have Thk Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers , with whom we do not wish to
interfere.

to be addressed to William Maclbod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Art icles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, arc specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 24, 1895.
The Exemption Clause.
When the Land Tax Bill makes its reap-
pearance in New South Wales Parliament
the great struggle, it is already obvious, will
turn on the proposed exemption of holdings
up to the value of £475. The Upper House
has already given its voice decisively against
the exemption, and professes its willingness
to pass the measure if made to apply equally
all round, but otherwise it threatens to
oppose it to the bitter end. The Daily
Telegraph —the recognised organ of the
Reid Government—also opposes the exemp-
tion. So did Mr. B. R. Wise, and so,
also, does part, at all events, of the
Opposition. The Premier formerly expressed
himself as absolutely neutral, if not actively
hostile, but since the majority of the last
Assembly insisted on inserting an exemption
clause he has declared himself a warm
adherent of the principle. The majority of
the present Assembly is also believed to be
in its favor. As for the nation, its opinion
on the subject is doubtful; it declared, in a
tolerably detinite way, in favor of the
Land-tax principle, but a general election
is a vague and unsatisfactory process, and,
at the best, it gives but a glimmering as
to the popular sentiment. The probabilities
are that the community has no definite
opinion on the exemption clause. A
desire to tax the other man is a con-
spicuous feature in everybody’s character.
Therefore the majority of the community
believes in a Land-tax because it owns no
land, and the great body of small land-
holders believe in a land-tax with the pro-
posed margin because it will itself go free.
But it is not all likely that the non-land-
holder cares whether the small owner is free
or not. And it is certain that the large
land-holder wants the tax to apply equally
allround, in the hope hit the small holder
will lose his love for the new impost when
he feels in his own person what it is like.
• x • #
On this question The Bulletin gives its
vote unhesitatingly with the Reid party. It
regards the Land-tax Bill as a somewhat
clumsy and ill-constructed affair, inasmuch
as it recognises no gradations, and taxes the
owner with £lO,OOO worth of land at the
same rate as the one with £lOO 000 worth.
Also, it draws no distinctions between the
man who cultivates or occupies his own laud,
and the one who sublets it and lives on the
rent, and the other one who neither culti-
vates nor occupies nor allows anyone else to
occupy, but holds the land for purely specu-
lative purposes, and thereby blocks settle-
ment and becomes a public evil. But while
the omission of the graduation principle
does much to destroy the purpose —or
what ought to be the purpose—of the
measure, the excision of the exemption
clause would only alter it still further
for the worse. The Bulletin takes this
view fully recognising that the public has
never had a chance—and cannot have one
except through the medium of the Referen-
dum—to fully express its opinion on the
subject, and also recognising that it may
have, at present, no very definite opinion to
express. Also, it recognises quite as fully
that the small land-holder may, under certain
conditions, be much richer and much better
able to pay a Land-tax than the large one,
and that the person who owns no laud at all
may be a richer capitalist than either of
them.
• « «
In this paper’s view, the Land-tax is not a
gospel, but an expedient. Gospels are
mostly extinct nowadays, and whoever at-
tempts to uphold any political creed, whether
it be Siugle-tax, Freetrade, Prohibition,
Protection, or any other, on the ground that
it is a creed to be followed for its own sake
and to be bolstered up with quotations from
the apostles and the prophets, is mostly
sawing the empty air. The case for a tax
which will apply equally to all land-holders,
large and small, is largely based on the
theory that the private ownership of land is
in itself a sin, and should be visited in each
case with the same penalty. The Bulletin
cares very little whether it is a sin or not.
What it considers is the obvious fact that in
a country of large estates there are a handful
of enormously rich land holders at one end
of the social scale, and at the other a vast mass
of poverty, misery, ignorance, serfdom, and
general discontent, and if the Australian
nation developes along these, lines it will
throw away the greatest chance which any
new community ever possessed. Therefore,
it advocates the State ownership of all lands,
simply in order that all tiie members of the
community may be something like equal
partners in the principal source of national
wealth. But if the same result, or some-
thing approaching it, can be arrived at by
other means, the means themselves matter
comparatively little. If it is possible to
create a system of small independent owner-
ship, so that every man will have a reason-
able chance to own something like his share
of the soil, the result will be much the same.
In neither case can absolute justice ever be
arrived at. If the State owns all the sur-
face of the province it can never, by any
possible process, fix the rents with mathe-
matical accuracy so as to annex all the un-
earned increment which the people create
—all the wisdom of all the Solons
would be insufficient for such a task And
if all the rents of all the lands in the country
flow into the Treasury, and are expended
for public purposes, no human wisdom will
ever distribute them so that every member
of the community will benefit equally, and
hardly anybody is honest enough to attempt
doing so. Similarly, it is possible to make
laws which will tend to the steady sub-
division of the soil down to a certain point,
but though the distribution can thus be
made much more equal than it now is, it
can never be made wholly accurate. Under
these circumstances, The Bulleiin has no
gospel to offer—only an expedient.
* * *
The reasons why the Legislative Council
urges a Land-tax without exemptions are
sufficiently obvious. (1) It will produce
more revenue in the aggregate, the extra
revenue being exacted from the poor—whom
the Council most emphatically does not
represent. (2) Because of this extra revenue
there will be all the less reason to increase
the tax hereafter. (8) The impost will fall
heavily on the small selector, almost the
poorest, hardest-worked, and most hopeless
member of the community, and will force
him to make common cause with the syndi-
cate and the big private monopolist in
demanding its reduction or abolition, or at
least in doing his level best to prevent any
future increase. (4) It will discredit the
Land-tax principle, as showing in an odious
light the conduct of the merchants, impor-
ters, manufacturers, shopkeepers, and all the
great crowd of more or less prosperous non-
laud-holders, even down to the city artisans,
who shift part of the burden of taxation off
their own shoulders to lay it on those of the
struggling selector, who is often less able to
bear it than any of them. And (5) it will
facilitate, rather than prevent, the accumula-
tion of large estates. This latter is a mere
matter of arithmetic. Many a small holder,
whose share of the tax would amount to, say,
£2 per annum, makes in a bad season or a
year of low prices perhaps £7O for his net
income, and, after paying the tax, would
have £6B left. And as the large owner, who
had an income of £7OO from rent and paid
perhaps £4O in taxes, would have £660 left,
lie would evidently be much better able to
stand the strain. This last feature of the
case is more speculative than the others, but
in the long run it would probably work out
in the direction indicated.
The only really important reason to be
advanced in favoring a land-tax without
exemption or gradation is that it would be a
convenient source of revenue. Beyond that
there is nothing to be said in its favor. It
would descend equally on the rich and the
poor, and would bear heavily on the agri-
culturist—the person who is, or should be,
the main support of the State. It would
not get at the unearned increment in any
equitable sense, for it would fall alike on the
man who had bought land at £lO an acre
and seen it rise, without any exertion on his
part, to £lOO, and on the man who had
bought at £3OO an acre sqid seen it fall to
£lOO. It would not discourage large monopo-
lies, or answer any purpose which could not
be answered almost equally well by a poll-
tax or a bread-tax, or any other revenue-
raising expedient. If the sole purpose in
view is to produce an income, then this sys-
tem is undoubtedly commendable, but if the
object is to produce income and at the same
time to exercise some kind of beneficial
effect on the community it has hardly a
single recommendation. At all events, the
same result could be arrived at by a property-
tax in any form.
# • •- _
The object to be attained is to encourage
a race of small independent land-holders,
each living on his own demesne, in prefer-
ence to a vast array of tenants bossed by an
arrogant squirearchy. New South Wales
lias been moving towards this latter destina-
tion for many years past. In 1880, small
holdings—those under 201 acres in extent —
constituted about one-twelfth of the alienated
lauds of the province ; in 1894 they consti-
tuted less than one-twentieth. In the for-
mer year, holdings varying from 201 to 1000
acrea comprised nearly one-fourth of the
total area ; in the latter, they formed about
one-sixth. In 14 years, the total area of the
estates under 201 acres in dimensions
increased by 300,000 acres ; while that
of the estates comprising more than 10,000
acres each increased by 10,800,000 —-
or, roughly speaking, 36 times as fast.
The total area alienated increased by
19,300,000 acres in 14 years, and the very
biggest estates, those of 10,000 acres and
upwards, account for a good deal more than
half of this sum. One-eighth of the whole
alienated area of New South Wales is now
occupied by tenants who pay rent to the
incipient squirearchy aforementioned. The
exemption proposed under the Land-tax Bill
is intended to meet this’ evil ; it will only do
it in a small degree at the best, but still it
will have a definite inclination in the right
direction. Assuming that land yields five
per cent., or Is. in the £ per annum on its
nominal value, a Land-tax of one penny in
the £ means the permanent confiscation of
one-twelfth part of the profits, and reduces
the permanent value of the property in the
same proportion. Therefore, on a vast
estate where the land was formerly worth
£1 an acre it will be worth, under the Land-
tax, only 18s. 4d. But on a small property,
which is below the exemption limit, pro-
vided it is fixed at, say, £475 or £5OO, it
will retain its former value. As a natural
consequence, the property which is worth
18s. 4d. an acre when held as one large taxed
estate can be made worth £1 an acre by sell-
ing it in small lots to individual holders, and
the small farm which is now worth £1 an
acre will have its value reduced to 18s. 4d.
when absorbed into the big estate adjoining.
The result will be an increasing tendency to
sub-division, and a decreasing tendency
towards aggregation. The alleged injustice
to the rich landlord, in the agricultural dis-
tricts at all events, is more real than apparent.
He can escape the impost at any time by
selling out in small lots, and letting all
future unearned increment be divided
throughout the nation, and this is not much
to ask of him, considering the favorable
treatment he has received in the past.
Chinese Missions.
Apt corroboration of The Bulletin’s views
regarding Chinese missions is found in a book
to hand by a recent London mail —Dr. G. E.
Morrison’s “ An Australian in China.” Dr.
Morrison is son to Dr. Geo. Morrison, of
Geelong (Vic.) College, and is the young man
who tramped across Australia from Carpentaria
to Melbourne, and told the story in Melb. Age.
His book is about a trip made from Shanghai
up the Yang-tse-kiang as far as Chungking,
whence he tramped it to the Burma frontier.
He knew no Chinese, had no interpreter, and
had for companions only low-caste coolies, and
be made the journey in Chinese dress, with a
pigtail fastened inside his hat, and without
arms. His very interesting book of travel-notes
will help Australian readers to get rid of many
false impressions. He says himself: “ I went
to China possessed with the strong racial anti-
pathy to the Chinese common to in/country-
men, but that feeling has long since given way
to one of lively sympathy and gratitude, and I
shall always look back with pleasure to this
journey, during which I experienced, while
traversing provinces as wide as European king-
doms, uniform kindness and hospitality, and
the most charming courtesy. In my case, at
least, the Chinese did not forget their precept,
‘ Deal gently with the stranger from afar.’ ”
* * •
Dr. Morrison’s experience only confirms
what is, for those who cave to know it, a well-
known fact— that is, that in every part of the
vast empire of China a foreigner is at least as
safe as a Chinaman is in, say, Australia or
America —if only he will refrain from needlessly
irritating native beliefs and prejudices. Sup-
pose a Chinese missionary came to Australia,
collected an audience, and declared to them that
their fathers and mothers were in hell, that the
head of their religion was in h 11, and that if
they did not at once abjure all their old beliefs
and espouse his new one they would infallibly
go to hell, too. How would that missionary be
likely to fare if his audience, for example, was
composed of ardent Wesleyans with a sprink-
ling of hard-shell Baptists and Salvationists ?
Would he escape with a sound hone in his
body? Yet some European missionaries in
China are guilty of an exactly similar outrage
on native feelings—intensified by the fact that
the Chinaman invests his parents with a sacred
character hardly intelligible by foreigners. Dr.
Morrison quotes B. Broomhall, secretary of
the China Inland Mission :
The millions of Chinese (who have never heard the
Gospel), what is to be their condition beyond the grave?
Oh, tremendous question 1 It is an awful thing to con-
template—hut they perish ; that is what God says.—
“ Evangelisation of the World," p. 70.
* # *
“Ancestral worship,” says Dr. Morrison,
“ is the keystone of the religion of the Chinese ;
‘ the keystone also of China’s social fabric.’
And 1 the worship springs,’ says Rev. W. A. P.
Martin, D. 1)., LL.D., of the Tung Wen Col-
lege, Peking, ‘ from some of the best principles
of human nature. The first conception of a life
beyond the grave was, it is thought, suggested
by a desire to commune with deceased parents.’
(‘ The Worship of Ancestors—a plea for tolera-
tion.’) But Dr. Hudson Taylor, the distin-
guished ..founder, of the -ChinaMnlund; Mission,
condemned bitterly this plea for toleration.
‘ Ancestral worship,’ he said at the Shanghai
Missionary Conference of May, 1890, ‘ is idola-
try from beginning to end, the whole of it and
everything connected with it.’ China’s religion
is idolatry, the Chinese are universally idola-
trous, and the fate that befalls idolaters is care-
fully pointed out by Dr. Taylor —‘ Their part
is in the lake of fire.’ ”
# * *
Yet people wonder that the Chinese refuse
the consolations which a belief that their friends
and relations are burning in hell-fire is so
eminently calculated to bestow ! The faith of
the missionaries (whom Dr. Morrison invari-
ably found occupying the most comfortable
houses in every town, but chiefly collected
round the treaty ports, where there is no risk
and plenty of pleasant society) is an astounding
thing to contemplate. Says Dr. Morrison :
Dining the time 1 was in China I met large numbers of
missionaries of all classes, in many, cities from Peking to
Canton, and they unanimously expressed satisfaction at
the progress they are making in China. Expressed
succinctly, their harvest may he descrihtdas amounting
to a fraction more than two Chinamen per missionary per
annum. If, however, the paid ordaimd and unordained
native helpers be added to the number of missionaries,
you find that the aggregate body converts nine tenths
of a Chinaman, per worker per annum ; but the mission-
aries deprecate their work being judged by statistics.
There are 1511 Protestant missionaries laboring in the
Empire; and, estimating their results from the statistics
of previous years as published in the Chinese Recorder,
«e find that they gathered last year (1893) into the fold
8127 Chinese—not all of whom it is fear dare genuine
Christians —at a cost of £350,000, a sum equal to the com-
bined incomes of the ten chief London hospitals.
Niue-tenths of a Chinaman per annum for
every worker in the vineyard ! At that rate,
supposing the numbers of Chinese and mission-
aries to remain stationary, the whole Chinese
nation (barring the few thousand millions who
die before the glad tidings have a chance of
reaching them) will be more or less converted
in exactly 100,000 years. Glorious prospect!
• • #
Consider the amazing variety of hunters for
Chinese soul-scalps. At Hankow (where “every
visitor is pleased to find that his preconceived
notions as to the hardships and discomforts of
the open-port missionary in China are entirely
false”) the Chinaman who clings to the religion
of his ancestors has to dodge missionaries of
the following denominations ;
London Missionary Society ; Tract Society; Local Tract
Society ; British and Foreign Bible Society; National
Bible Society of Scotland ; American Bible Society ;
private Quaker, Baptist, Wesleyan, and Independent mis-
sionaries; Church Missionary Society; American Board
of Missions ; American High Church Episcopal Mission ;
French Mission ; Franciscan Mission (Italian) ; Augustine
Mission (Spanish); Scandinavian Mission; Danish Mis-
sion ; and China Inland Mission.
These all preach more or less different brands
of gospel; and each, for the Chinaman, repre-
sents the emissary of a different god. And yet
the nett result for all emissaries of all gods is
only nine-tenths of a convert per emissary per
annum ! Blind, ignorant, besotted China ! A
little further inland, at Wanhsien, there is a
branch of the China Inland Mission, established
since 1887.
There are, unfortunately, no converts, but there are
three hopeful “enquirers,” whose conversion would be
the more speedy the more likely they were to obtain
employment ufeerwards. They argue"m this way; they
say, to quote the words used by Ihe Rev. G. L. Mason, at
the Shanghai Missionary Conference of 1890, “if the
foreign teacher will lake care of our bodies we will do him
the favor to seek the salvation of our souls.” “ The idea
is universally prevalent in China,” says the Rev, C. VV.
Mateer, “ that everyone who enters any sect should live
by it. . . When a Chinaman becomes a Christian he
expects to live by his Christianity.”
Hence the numerous breed of “Rice Christians,”
to which probably 99 per cent, of all Chinese
converts belong. They are those who, in the
Chinese vernacular, “love Jesus because they
eat Jesus’ rice.” Those who have rice of then-
own stay outside the fold and make sarcastic
remarks about the shepherds. Of one of the
threp Wanhsien missionaries who have preached
the Gospel with such success that they have
gained no converts in six years (The Bulletin
hopes it isn’t the one from Sydney), Dr. Mor-
rison remarks that nearly all the Chinese in
Wanhsien have heard his doctrine described
with greater or less unintelligibility, and it is
at their own risk if they still refuse to be
saved. The method of this particular soul-
catcher has been pleasantly likened by the
Chinese to that of “a blind fowl picking for
worms.” Brutal, debased China !
* * «
And this is the picture of Chinese mission
work which recurs in Dr. Morrison’s book
with dismal iteration. At Suifu—
There is an enthusiastic young missionary who in
formerly a French polisher in Hereford. He is helped bv
an amiable wife and by a charming English girl scarcely
out of her teens. The missionary’s work has, he tells me,
been “abundantly blessed he has baptised six converts
in three years.
Members of the China Inland Mission—
Six months after their arrival write to China’s Millions :
“ Now for the news ! Glorious news this time ! Ours i-
vices crowded! Such bright, intelligent faces! Si
eager to hear the good news! They seemed to drink in
every word, and to listen as if they were afraid that a
word might be lost.” Five years later they write: "T .e
first convert in Siao Wong Miao was a young mar


named Tbnglkping, a mat-seller. He was very earnest in
his efforts to spread the Gospel, but about the beginning
. tlie vear he became insane. The poor man lost his
reason, but not his piety. "-China's Millions, iv., 5, 95
and 143.
At Yunnan the mission is
Conducted by Mr. and Mrs. X., assisted by Mr. Gra-
ham and by two exceedingly nice young girls, one of
whom comes from Melbourne. . . . After Mr. X. has
labored here nearly six years, he has no male converts,
though there are two promising nibblers. . . . There
was a convert, baptised before Mr. X. came here, a poor
manure-coolie, who was employed by the mission as an
evangelist in a small way ; but “ Satan tempted him, he
fell from grace, and had to be expelled for stealing the
children’s buttons.” It was a sad trial to the mission.
The men refuse to be saved, recalcitrant sinners ! but the
women happily are more tractable. Mr. X. has up to
date (May, ’94) baptised his children’s nurse-girl, the
“native helper” of the single ladies, and his wife’s cook.
Three “rice Christians” for six years’ work!
Obstinate China ! In the whole of the pro-
vince of Yunnan are 18 Protestant missionaries,
who have opened since ’Bl five stations, and
have altogether achieved 11 converts. Says
Dr. Morrison :
The problem is this: In a population of from five to
seven millions of friendly and peaceable people, 18 mis-
sionaries in eight years (the average time during which
the mission stations have been opened) have converted 11
Chinese: how long, then, will it take to convert the re-
mainder?
And the missionaries echo, “How long, O
Lord, how long ? ”
* * *
These are samples of the facts and statistics
with which Dr. Morrison’s book bristles. And
he is only one of the innumerable cloud of wit-
nesses who make it clear that the British Bible,
like British opium, is thrust upon the Chinese
at the point of the bayonet; that the chief
result of missionary work in China is the pro-
vision of a comfortable living for the treaty-
port shepherds—only the women and zealots
going to take risks inland ; that the other
results are the debasement of a few Chinese, the
occasional massacre of a missionary or two, and
the subsequent massacre of many Chinese to
glut the revenge of the followers of Him who
said : “ Lord, forgive them ; they know not
what they do ; ” and that the whole business is
the natural harvest of a diseased fanaticism,
which now, as in all ages, is never content till
the sacrifices to its idol are soaked in human
blood.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Advance Westralia!
In the West, last month, the cause of Austra-
lasian progress achieved, almost unnoticed, a
signal triumph.. Every province but Westralia
had practically decreed a divorce between State
and Church, and now Westralia comes into line.
The circumstances give remarkable illustration
of the power of public opinion over public men.
Agitation for theabolitionof denominational edu-
cation in Westralia commenced two years ago,
but it gathered weight like a rolling snowball.
Bitterly the hoary old Conservatives, the Rip Van
Winkles of the continent, who cluster most
thickly on the western seaboard, fought against
it in pulpit and press, on platform and hust-
ings. Fiercely it was opposed by the unscru-
pulous Forrest Govt., the despotic nepotists
who have cemented blood and bigotry into the
strongest partisan alliance in Australasian
politics. But with the advent of responsible
government came a widening of the fran-
chise which let the light into many a
mouldy political sepulchre, and rattled
the bones of the old Tories as those
venerable relics had never been rattled before.
The gold discoveries at Coolgardie and the
Murchison brought over a swarm of the hated
“ t’other-siders,” hot-foot in chase of the magic
metal, brimful of eastern energy and eastern
ideas. The transformation was as sudden as
that which the stream of Californian gold-
seekers wrought on the sleepy “pure merinoes ”
who peopled N.S. Wales and Victoria in the
fifties. Westralia woke up. And, as soon
as Democracy found articulate voice, it
began to repeat the truisms familiar “on
the other side ” that the State, sup-
ported by the contributions of all the tax-
payers, should not devote those contributions
to spreading the religious opinions of any par-
ticular body of taxpayers; that it was the
State’s business to fit its children to become
good citizens on earth, but not to train them to
be good angels in heaven ; that State patronage
of religious schools poisoned the fount of
citizenship at the springs, and commenced
in childhood a strife which would embitter
maturity. During every recent session of
Parliament the question of abolishing
State aid to denominational schools has
been discussed, and always with increasing
vigor on the part of the democrats, with
iitriinishing opposition on the part of the
sectarians. Late in July the crisis came. A
lirect want of confidence motion was levelled
igainst the Govt., and the Govt.’s heart failed
it. “Don’t shoot, Colonel,” said John For-
rest—and came down. The Qovt. gave an
unconditional guarantee that the denomina-
tional system should be ended ; and the motion
was withdrawn. The rest is only a matter of
time.
“ Stiffening ” the Tote.
A bill to legalise the totalisator has been
utterly rejected by the Victorian Assembly
under circumstances which, to every well-
informed member of the racing world, appear
scandalous. The Bill in question was no new
scheme of turf improvement. Born in Maori-
land, and afterwards adopted by South Aus-
tralia, it made a preliminary appeal to the
honest intelligence of the Victorian Assembly
exactly three years ago, when it passed a third
reading by 31 votes to 24. Up to that time the
local bookmakers had seen no cause for alarm,
and their power to bend the high court of Par-
liament to their will was not exercised in any
degree worth mentioning. A steady decline in
gate-money receipts during the next two years
brought the totalisator question into notice by
V.R.C. committeemen and others interested in
restoring the ancient fortunes of racing-clubs.
Conservative stewards began to partially re-
cognise three facts, now that they could no
loneer afford to ignore the real state of affairs.
“ In and out ” running of horses was killing the
sport; these fatal malpractices were mainly
brought about by bookmakers ; the introduc-
tion of the tote would go far towards killing
the “ hooks,” and put new life into the rescued
sport. The big racing-club managers had not yet
decided to throw over their old friend, who may
here be generically alluded to as Ikey
Mo, but they showed symptoms of a new regard
for the tote. Whereupon Ikey made himself
busy among the senators. In July, 1894, the
Tote Bill was quietly pitched out of the Assem-
bly by 28 votes to 16. Gate-money receipts
dropped more and more, stakes fell in value
accordingly, and racing was degenerating into a
second-class sport. The members of the V. R.C.
desired their committee to take a vote upon the
now burning question of a legalised totalisator,
and the committee held up their bands with an
overwhelming majority. This happened quite
recently. Meanwhile Ikey Moses and Co. had
made arrangements to corrupt every portion of
the senate open to corruption, either by cash
down, the cancelling of hook-debts, or other-
wise. A month ago, before the Lower House
had been properly surveyed, Ikey Mo feared
that he would fail to prevent Murray’s
Bill from reaching the Council. It was on the
Council that Ikey fixed his sinister eye, and
prepared to shed his specie. But a month is a
long time in Victorian politics. Ere the Tote
Bill was offered to the Assembly, its fate under-
went a terrible change for the worse. Ikey
Mo “stiffened” it. In his pardonable exulta-
tion, he must have got slightly unsober. His
tongue was loosened. The news that the Tote
Bill had been “got at” travelled around the
inner circle of sporting Melbourne, and tight-
trousered philosophers, with straws in their
mouths, remarked that “ the books deserved
the win, for they worked very ’ard for it ”
although the tote debate had not been broached
at the time. The present Vic. Assembly con-
tains 23 members who voted for the tote in days
of yore and 25 who voted against it. In the
ordinary course of things those 23 old supporters
of the machine would have swollen to at least
50 last week. Many false arguments, such as
the venerable lie that all horseowners and
trainers hold totes in abomination, are no longer
available, although they formed the back-bone
of Ikey-Moism three years ago. Some of the
25 old opponents of rational betting legislation
know more of their subject than they knew
last time, and among the 47 members who
hadn’t voted either way there must be a fair
percentage of feeling growing feeling in
favor of the tote. About 62 members rolled up
to decide the fate of Ikey Mo.’s betenoir last
week—the biggest muster on record ! When
the father of the Tote Bill called for a
division he found himself with twelve sup-
porters and “about 50 ” opponents, so he “with-
drew his demand for a division,” and Ikey Mo
had “a walkover.” This is a disgusting incident
to reflect upon, but not more disgusting than
other incidents relating to gambling that have
happened in the Vic. Assembly within modern
times. Anyway, Ikey Mo’s victory was not
accomplished until J. B. Patterson had swal-
lowed a pill. Patterson is one of those wdio
support Ikey on the ground that betting ought
to be discouraged and “ put down” to a great
extent. Scott, M.L.A., pointed out that the
aforesaid Patterson steadfastly declined to
put down certain financial gamblers when he
had the chance, and was the protector of two
classes of men who prey upon the public. It
was a very timely attack. Three brands of
legislators vote for Ikey Mo. The respectable
old hands who defend all bad institutions on
principle, the pious men who follow their un-
enlightened consciences in such matters, and
the patriots whom Ikey “stiffens.”
Bogus Socialism.
State Socialism has one cloven hoof and one
sound one, and the cloven hoof was thrust out
prominently in Sydney the other day. Gener-
ally speaking, State Socialism may be defined
as a mean between the objectionable system of
individualism, which permits everybody to do
what is right in his own eyes and cordially
agrees to the devil collaring the hindmost, and
the equally objectionable system of absolute
collectivism, which would leave nobody any
personal freedom at all, but would sort
all citizens into companies and regiments
drilled, under severe penalties, to do the
bidding of a bureaucracy. The Bulletin
believes in State Socialism, because it believes
that, under the present constitution of society,
there are many things which can be better done
by the community, acting for all its members,
than by every individual acting for himself.
No community can exist without inter-
fering with the liberty of its members
to some extent, and it often has to
interfere to a very great extent, despite the
shrieks of many who are revered as the most
upright pillars which any Church can obtain.
John Bright, for example, could never be
brought to see that the State had any busi-
ness to say that children employed in his
factories should not work 16 hours a day ; and
John Bright spelt conscience with a large C,
and peace with a large P, and in many ways
posed as a first-class philanthropic patriot.
But the British nation didn’t happen to agree
with John Bright, and the Factory Acts duly
became law. On the other hand, when the
Paris Commune started out to nationalise the
other fellow’s dining-room furniture, and wood
and coal, and six-roomed cottages, the French
nation seriously objected, and the Commune
was promptly suppressed. So that it is plain
that national sentiment does not always agree
with individual sentiment as to whether State
Socialism is going too far, or not far enough.
Quite possibly the steps which it takes
at one time, or in one set of circumstances,
may appear utterly wrong to public opinion
expressed at another time, or in another
set of circumstances. Public opinion in Aus-
tralia seems, on the whole, satisfied that
the Post-office and the railways and a few
other things should be run by the State;
and The Bulletin is trying to make people
see that a State Bank, and State Life In-
surance, and a State Grog Department,
and two or three similar incursions into the
domain of “ private enterprise,” would also be
to their advantage. But every socialistic in-
stitution must stand and deliver an answer to
the question : Are its operations in the public
■interest? Now, Sydney Post-office, following
the example of State post-offices all the world
over, has just secured damages from a privately-
owned express-company for interfering with
its monopoly by carrying letters from one
city address direct to another for a fee
of sixpence. There is no doubt that the public
want letters so carried ; no doubt that the Post-
office supplies no facilities for carrying them.
The Post-office bureaucrats, therefore, say in
effect to the public: “Your interests conflict
with our regulations, therefore your interests
must give way. You are created for our
benefit : not we for yours. And as you have
been fools enough to make it criminal for any
man to give another man money to carry a
letter for him, you shall be punished accord-
ingly.” Which is a neat reduction to absurdity
of the socialistic dogma.
There are few human exhibitions more hideous
than those of the alleged “reformed sinners”
who mount moral platforms in order to adver-
tise present purity by contrast with former
depravity. The ex-profligate, or burglar, or
confidence-man, who screams about his un-
savory past, wears the offhand aspect of still
hungering after the fleshpots of uncleanness.
Real repentant shame would hide its past from
all unnecessary advertisement. The three re-
deemed gamblers—an ex-bcokie, an ex-jockey,
and an ex-toteclerk—who gave zest to a so-called
anti-gambling demonstration in Sydney on a
recent Sunday, proved no more against gamb-
ling than could be proved against the use of
wine and other stimulants, as distinguished
from abuse. Everybody who plans his conduct
one instant in advance gambles for that in-
stant with eternity—inasmuch as he risks the
present moment, the only dead certainty, against
one in the future which it is any odds from the
infinitesimal to the infinite may (for him) never
arrive. And the churchmen who condemn
gambling are the worst gamblers of all, for
they hand over all this world’s pleasures in cash
to an almighty bookmaker, on His alleged credit
note guaranteeing eternal happiness if they can
win the race to Heaven—in which, on their own
showing, the odds are millions to one against
them.
Confucianism, Buddhism, and other forms of belief,
have set up in their centres of influence religious cancers,
the paralysis of caste, and polygamy ; but the gospel of
Christianity has produced health and nothing but health,
has brought on no paralysis, not even discolored the com-
plexion or dimmed the eyes of any- State, community, or
individual that has carried it into practice.— Rev. Joseph
Cook.
A mere begging of the question ; because, not
one State, not one community, and not one
individual, except Christ Himself, has ever
carried the Gospel of Christianity into prac-
tice. The cant of Cook and others that
usurp the name of Christians ignores the
very essence, of their alleged creed,
and, while mouthing, repudiates by hourly
action the “ Sermon on the Mount.” Alleged
Christianity has upheld, and in places still
upholds, “the Divine right of Kings” and a
“ privileged aristocracy ’’—which is caste.
Alleged Christianity everywhere bows down
and worships the moneyocracy—which is also
caste. Alleged Christianity charges pew-rents
—the worst form of caste. Even Joe Cook
preaches the “ glorious Gospel ” for hard.cash
and charges money at the door. Alleged Chris-
tianity exalts the godly brewer and the pious
distiller, and breeds drunkenness. Mahommed-
auism scores a record there. As to polygamy,
if polygamy is a cancer, monogamy is too often
a brutally slow torture and final murder.
Alleged Christianity has pretended to abolish
slavery, but it holds human flesh and blood to
be a mere chattel of the rich : alleged Chris-
tianity swears by “ freedom of contract ” under
compulsion—a paradox in term, but the worst
sort of slavery in fact.
Dear Bulletin,— “ What may happen to a
man in Victoria.” I am arrested on a charge,
the truth of which I deny, and am remanded
from week to week while the police scour the
province, and use the immense resources at
their disposal to obtain evidence against me ;
me—a single individual who am all this while
locked up, allowed to see a visitor (satisfactory
to the gaol governor) twice a week, and per-
mitted to write two letters per week (ditto,
ditto). I must get up at certain hours and go
to bed ditto (6 a.m. to 4 p.m. ! in winter) must
wash myself daily once only, in second-hand
water, and stop in a very small yard all
day doing nothing. I may not smoke ; I must,
on no account, see any newspapers, nor am I
allowed to have even the solace—if such it be
—of the religious magazines allowed to con-
victed prisoners. 1 am allowed to have my
meals sent in from outside provided I do so
from the first; should I take hominy even once,
through ignorance or inability to make arrange-
ments in time, then it is hominy to the end.
The food sent in is overhauled by a “ searcher”
—not a warder, but a “billeted” prisoner—and
—well, good care is taken by him that I shall
not grow fat from overfeeding. I am not even
allowed to have a bath, no matter how long I
may be under remand (or committal), without
asking the governor for it at the usual morning
“inspection.” And, finally, should I eventually
be convicted, not one hour of the time spent
under remand counts in the sentence, which
begins only with the first day of the Sessions at
which one is convicted. To take a so far innocent
man from every-day life and straightway shut
him up day and night without papers, books,
tobacco, and very often without even the society
of another or others similarly situated, seems
to trie to be a first-class plan of breaking a man
down physically and mentally—whifch is ap-
parently what the authorities desire.— British
Injustice.
—
One of these days the Victorian worker may
ask John Hancock, M.L.A., for reasons why
he objects to the tote, and is even recognised as
a leading champion of Ikey Mo. If Hancock’s
constituents were grand-standers at Fleming-
ton, which they are not, they would pretty
often find John prowling round the betting-
ring in search of political wisdom. So it isn’t
on religious grounds that the vehement repub-
lican opposes machine betting. The writer
has once or twice’ seen him holding
what looked like a council of war pre-
vious to backing a horse with a bookmaker
for the next race, and he shouldn’t need tell-
ing that the tote would lay longer odds.
Furthermore, he can’t cherish much natural
love and affection for Ikey, who starves
the prices, or else he wouldn’t be a friend
of the poor and oppressed. But, stay !
The employment of as much labor as pos-
sible at the highest possible rates is Han-
cock’s worthy aim. The tote would throw a
lot of Ikey Mo. out of employment, and the
balance of that toiling Hebrew identity would
be indirectly “ sweated ” by the machine.
Therefore, Hancock is consistent in opposing
the cupidity of Ikey’s employer, the Public.
He will probably organise a bookmakers’ trade-
union, and next 21st of April, perhaps, John
will be seen mounted on a stiffened steed at the
head of a band of bloated proteges, bearing a
little banner inscribed “ A Living Wage for the
Wagerer. Back your Choice with the Chosen
Chowser ! ”
A new variety of Protection was proposed in
Vic. Assembly t’other night. The trade in
local fruits is said to suffer from the competi-
tion of nigger-grown bananas and pineapples,
and Levien, M.L.A., asked for a duty of Is.
per bushel upon those toothsome products of
Nature and cheap labor, not so much for
revenue purposes, as in order to work a
patriotic change in the national diet. Some-
how the idea didn’t quite catch on. Tbenwith
couldn’t see that a banana and pineapple tax
would lure the public on to apples, or enlarge
the local labor market, anyway. Also, he sug-
gested that prohibition of fruits not grown
in Victoria might pave the way for an Act
compelling the population to live exclusively
on wheat, for the sake of the country ; like-
wise, he raised the technical objection that
bananas are not imported by the bushel. After
which it occurred to somebody else that a re-
taliatory tax upon Victorian cherries exported
to Bananaland would possibly set up a foreign
prejudice against stone-fruit. The matter then
dropped with a sickening thud, and the Mel-
bourne price of the mail-clad pineapple will
remain as before. It goes without saying that'
Levien, M.L.A., belongs to the anti-Protec-
tiouist persuasion. Anything exceptionally
hopeless in the guise of a protective motion is
bound to come from a Freetrader.
The Rescue, a serious little paper representing
the pious and teetotal interests, and having its
address at the Temperance Hall, Sydney, pub-
lishes a few remarks on “the present crisis.”
An extract:
Social position and title from the Queen downward is
sneered at, and the hereditary chamber of this great
empire, the House of Lords, is threatened with overthrow
by men who ought to know and do know better. The
people are determined to rule. . . . Government by
many is a huge blunder. . . . How can you expect
laws to be made and obeyed by the self-same persons?
. . . Socialism, the hand-maid of atheism, is rampant
in this country and on the continent. It saps the founda-
tion of morality, corrupts individual and corporate con-
science. It ruthlessly tramples on the rights of capital
and class privilege. Socialism never gives; it demands.
It is destructive, never constructive. One broadly-
marked feature of political life is the absence of indepen-
dent thought and action. All political parties are alike
in the eager bid for the solid Labor-vote. A Government
put into power by the popular will is one of inherent
weakness ; and its tenure of office being dependent on the
capricious will of the people must necessarily be a brief
one.
And yet in almost every country where the
people govern Mr. Ardill, of the Rescue, can
whoop to his heart’s content in perfect safety,
while in China, where the horrible experiment
of popular Government has never been heard
of, he would probably be clawed into small
pieces, and in Turkey he might be swamped in
some Armenian massacre, and in Russia he
would quite possibly be sent to Siberia. Why
doesn’t Mr. Ardill go and live under one of
these beautiful despotisms ?
If there is ordinary meaning to be attached to
common English, and if the daily-paper report
is a fair one, then the solemn argument of Rev.
J. I). Langley, a recently-promoted Anglican
archdeacon in Sydney, and that of Congrega-
lionalist parson Dunstan, is that God answered
Card. Moran’s Romish “foul aspersion ” on
Protestant foreign missions, in respect of their
“ comfort and comparative luxury,” by inspir-
ing the savage hearts of the Chinese to butcher
the Protestant missionaries ! Strip this state-
ment naked, and behold a stupendous and
blasphemous libel, made as by a privileged
confidant, on the Christians’ Almighty God.
Further analysed, the archdeacon’s argument
stands as a monumental illustration of the
fanatical fact that nothing is too harrowing,
nothing too hallowed, to be used as a merciless-
bludgeon on the head of a sectarian rival, even
though that rival.be a brother-worshipper of
the same Christ. If Card. Moran wants tc
“heap coals of fire on the heads” of his
theological enemies, he should send an ex
cathedra message of sympathy and condolence
to the bereaved Protestant missionary societies.
The humor of tragedies !
“ The defendant has been using the letters
L.R.C.P. after his name without being legally
entitled to do so ? ” asked a Lpndon magistrate
lately. “ Fined .£2O, with £lO 10s. costs, or
one month’s imprisonment.” Instead of strain-
ing after the unattainable or vexatiously de-
batable, N.S.W. Parliament should, when it
next takes up the question of quack regulation,
go for a short, straight bill, giving stipendiary
magistrates powers like the above. The man who
attaches to his name letters signifying that he is
what he isn’t, has no arguablp defence for his mis-
representation, and five minutes of Parliament’s
time should be ample for his suppression. He has
escaped so far, not because he has attempted
any defence, but because those who undertook
to legislatively suppress him persistently ex-
ceeded the scope of public requirement. They
wanted to extend a monopolistic influence or
otherwise aggrandise a constituted medical
guild at the same time that they assisted the
public to crush imposture, and the public has
in effect said, over and over again, that it
would rather the imposture should continue
than that these other ends should be attained.
It is threatened that, should the N,S. W,
land-tax pass, one of the immediate effects will
be a general reduction of the wages of station-
hands.

“ Gentleman Joe”—with Maud Hobson in
a snappy part us Missdu Potts, an American with
the usual twang at 40 h.p. ; Miss Grace Palotta,
as Mrs. Kalli Carr, a chaperone; and Bradfield in
the name-part, which lands him at Margate in a
comic suit of clothes in the last act—succeeds
‘‘The Shop Girl” at Sydney Lyceum to-night
(Saturday). “ Gentleman Joe ” will have to he a
naming red-and-rainbow novelty to wipe the flure
with “ The Shop Girl,” which ran three weeks to
excellent biz. (accelerated by Manager Goodman’s
convalescent smile on the door-mat), and finally
put up the shutters to exuberant houses. The
reason is not far to seek. The public certainly
got more Gee-Gee for its money in ,l The Shop
Girl ” than in any other show. The entire co.
disported itself in Mr. Hooley’s emporium, and
Lady Appleby's bazaar—the planetary system, as
you might say—was in perfect working order in
“The Shop Girl’’: all the stars in their places,
with thecheated under-studies grinding their teeth
in the disguise of “ guests ” ami “ foundlings, * far
from the centre of the stage. The Bulletin is
sorry to .say good-bye to “The Shop Girl”—it
was such a masterpiece of aimlessness.

Wm. Elton and “The Guv’nor” continue to
misunderstand each other to encouraging business
at Sydney Criterion, and the cantankerous ex-
pression on the mahogany visage of the deaf old
boatman, Theodore Macclesfield Elton, is alto-
f ether different to the amiable smile of plain \V,
Jiton, comedian, as he-spins a bicycle down Pitt-
street o’ mornings with a straw hat hooked on to
his bump of philoprogenitiveness. “The Guv’nor”
plays more smartly now than it did on its swear-
ing-in ; the more or ornamental members of
the co. display more alacrity in getting out of
Westmacott’s way when ho spurts for the wings
with his stutter in full blast; and the long,
spidery, water-side character in a blue jersey is
more punctual in colliding with the heir of the
jam-factory than he was at earlier dates—the
collision novy takes place in full view of the
audience, which formerly only heard the bump.
Miss Harrie Ireland (whose method strongly sug-
gests Miss Myra Kemble here and there) still
makes melodrama of her light comedy part ;
while Miss Madge Corcoran, on the other
hand, takes to the boards as timidly as a
oat to soapsuds— custom never seems to assure
this trim young lady of the fact that the
audience has not assembled for the express
purpose of eating her. But everybody is
well-placed in the “Guv'nor”—barring a long,
strange youth who wanders through the rattling
farce handicapped by an over-balancing moustache
and the depressing stage-name of “Bindloss.”

In “ The World Against Her ” Maud
Williamson holds feminine emotions in the hollow
of her little hand, judging by the generous display
of ’ankercbers in the family-circle when Madge
Carlton grabs her little Annie (clever little .Marie
Lohr) from one of the several bold bad men, and
dares them to part a mother from her che-yild.
Miss Williamson's forcible, though reserved, style
of acting, aided by her fine eyes and shapely figure,
carries a decidedly tin-pot domestic tragedy to a
successful conclusion. But in emotional rdles of
this brand Miss Williamson’s name is a guarantee
that no deep sob marked in the business will be
omitted by the leading woman, and that strict
attention will be paid to all the agony piled on by
the author. Scot luglis, as the villain, got npa /a
Henry Irving, omits neither eye-glass, evening
dress, the use of the word “damn,” nor cigar-
ette to make his wickedness convincing, though
his habit of grinding his teeth gives him°a
toothaohey appearance now and then. A
word of praise must be levelled at Mr. Leighton,
whose dude collar burst from its moorings oil
Monday night, at the critical moment when Miss
Claudius, in a dreamy confection like cabbages
veiled in coal-smoke, was leaning her ear on his
vest. But Mr. Leighton let the collar go, and
hugged the lady in the most natural manner in
the world. The weakest spot in “The World
Against Her ” was Mr. H. Plimmer, as the hero.
Mr. P. will never look like a grnwn-up man while
he oontinuesto pull such cry-baby faces, “Joseph
of Canaan” goes up to-night (Saturday), with
George Rignold as Joseph and Maud Williamson
as Mrs. Potiphar. This sounds most promising,
only that a managerial programme-foot-note rather
destroys the bouquet. “The Biblical foundation
of the play,” says the management, “cannot offend
the most scrupulous,” In which case it surely
can’t be an unabridged translation.

The Countess Waclitmeister, Mrs. Besant's
theosopbical understudy, has arrived in Sydney to
expound the gospel of the marvellous Mahatma,
who is so wise, and so pure, and so noble, that he
has to pass all his days in sad soliloquy on a Hima-
layan peak, for fear the skirts of his mystic gar-
ment would be contaminated if he came down
and jostled elbows with the wretched ruck of
humanity. Ihe Countess, a forlorn lady whose
utter abandonment to “ the Cause ” seem to have
washed out all the distinctive characteristics
the ever possessed, rather glides over the
Mahatma, especially the Judge brand of the
article, and confines herself to wandering
vaguely round among the mazes of theo-
sophical doctrine, and exhorting people to look
for the Bath, and choose the Way, and seek the
Bight, and generally let their benevolence ooze
out as much as ever it wants to. There is a bland,
soothing influence about the Countess's person-
ality that deprives one of the power of criti-
cising her capacity too severely, and if she
could be pounded up into small fragments and
boiled down to Extract of Wachtmeister, this
paper would willingly buy a small bottle as an
anodyne for its weary nerves,

The Tivoli’s novelty this week is a little
act entitled “After the Ball,” in which Miss
Kate Girard raises her foot and kicks a hat held
a very great distance above tier head, while
Mr. Emile Girard turns somersaults of admira-
tion for pure love of her. Evidently the ball-
supper was a very good one. The rest of the
show remains very much \\ here it was, only it
has glued itself down there even more deter-
minedly, and hammered several large tucks into
itself to keep itself from coining off.

At the Empire, the Mayfield sisters have
surrounded themselves with many thousand
yards of muslin, in which they play hide and
seek at intervals with great dexterity. The
remainder of the show is thrown into some
amount of temporary shade by these agile
apparitions, but the sweetly tuneful voice of
Miss Ada Colley pierces that shade with much
vigor.

If the “ memory-scientist,” Professor
Loisette, didn’t possess such a large number
of apparently convincing testimonials, Tmk
Bulletin would be disposed to set him down
as a very ordinary kind of itinerant Yankee
showman with nostrums of uncertain saving
power. As this paper hasn’t paid ,£5 ss. fur
the privilege of entering the Loisette holy of
holies, it can only express the opinion that
the outer tabernacle (admission, 25.) is stuffed
with witless jokes, stale pseudo-science, and an
exceedingly large number of dull and yellow
platitudes. Consequently, The Bulletin
has determined with much regret that when
it has saved up ,£5 ss. it will give not one
penny of it to Prof. Loisette on the off-chance
of being enabled to remember many things which
it wants badly to forget, but will buy “Stokes on
Memory ” fur a shilling and devote the balance to
the purchase of admission-tickets fur newer shows,
with a sufficient variety of legs in them to be
recollected without the least trouble.

The ill success of the B. and B. season at
Melb. Princess’ is partly chargeable to manager
Boucicault s method of employing Ids forces.
Miss Olliffe has, without a doubt, made herself a
special object of interest for the public, yet, in six
out of nine pieces produced during eleven weeks,
slie played either a minor part, or, more often,
didn’t appear at all. “Sowing the Wind ” offered
a favorable opportunity for displaying that strong,
magnetic, picturesque actress, but the part so well
adapted to her was given to the other lady,
Miss Falier. People wanted to see Miss Olliffe as
“ The Second Mrs. Tanqileray ’’—the certainty of
brisk demand for Pinero's masterpiece was ap-
parent long before the event, but when at last the
success of the season put in an appearance it
was restricted to a four nights’ run. “Johu-k-
Dreams ’ might not have proved such a very dire
failure if Miss Olliffe hail taken Miss Faber’s com-
manding place, instead of being left to assert her
ability in a very light-comedy character. The
managerial policy with regard to the Olliffe-
brauch of the business has a decidedly wavering
appearance. If it was good enough to engage an
able and fascinating leading lady to till Mrs.
Brough’s shoes for awhile, it should be good
enough to run her for all she is worth.

Musical Melbourne, which lay torpid for
years after swallowing the Halle entertainments,
sh >ws a disposition to consume some more masses
of food. On Saturday afternoon, Herr Johann
Kruse, lately back from Europe with a reputa-
tion, was announced to make a last appearance in
his native city, and his native city rushed the
Town Halj in order to catch a glimpse of its long-
lost offspring's reputation before he went. The
concert was a good concert in the same sense that
the Koran is a good book. Herr Kruse, unexcep-
tional, although not a marvellous, violinist, was
supported by that brilliant, non-severe piano
artist. Signor de Beanpuis. Also, the performance
of the audience in pretending to enjoy themselves
immensely was not less worthy of praise than the
efforts of Marshall Hall’s orchestra, in the back-
ground. The Krentzer Sonata for violin and
piano was the biggest tiling on the programme.
This was preluded by a silent procession of Mar-
shall Hall to a side gallery, where the professor
sat apart front the other men, with his back to the
wall, discovering new depths of meaning in each
note. The exact circumference of this item was
40m in.

Beethoven’s “Kreutzcr" is a comparatively
giddy piece of great-master music—a novelette, so
to i-peak. About 20 minutes of it are devoted to
introductory matter, or dry, irrelevant details,
which, in a written story, would be skipped by
the reader. The rest of the story is often delight-
ful. A strong imagination might almost picture
Edwin and Angelina looked in one another’s hug
when the music ceases. Neither Kruse nor De
Beanpuis had seemed quite at home with the
sonata, but they were vociferously applauded
until they took an encore. They is no skipping
the makeweight portions of a sonata, or violin
concerto. Saturday’s programme had to be sat out,
tlm Town Hall seats are hard and unsympathetic,
and popular enthusiasm was exhausted ere the
finish. A solo I»y Herr Kruse was the last num-
ber, and aa soon as the demigod of the occasion
had completed his task, the audience made for the
doors without pausing to give him a “hand.’’
The inconsistency of conduct was partly excus-
able. During the afternoon R. S. Smytlie
flooded the Ifall with circulars promising yet
another last Kruse conceit —at greatly reduced
prices. Therefore, the four or five hundred
pound-worth of people present, having paid high
prices for a performance which wasn’t valedictory
at all, felt that R.B.S. owed them something more
than mere gratitude.
* • •
A trophy for Kruse was dumped upon the
platform last Saturday at the inopportune moment
that comes between two movements. The trophy
was a withered-looking pot-plant in a wicker
stand, from which hung a gaudy sheet inscribed ;
“ Virginia Bower—Filial Love.” This aw ful
device for reminding the world that family troubles
had brought Kruse over from Germany made the
astonished artist blush, and a professional artist
doesn’t blush easily.

« * *
“ Saved from the Sea,” at Melb. Royal,
is one of those awful affairs in which the author
tries to blast the work of the scenic artist and
fails miserably. W. B. Spong has loaded up
Bland Holt’s latest triumph with landscapes such
as no evil influences can fight against: further-
more, there is a rolling deep in the first act which
would keep the leadenest of melodramas afloat.
The ocean stretches from Little Bourke-street to
the footlights, and gets as near to salt-water in
appearance as any reasonable mortal can expect.
Miss Watson and Walter Baker are discovered
breasting its cold, damp, wadding billows by the
light of arising moon until Mr. and Mrs. Bland Holt
come rocking along in a small boat and save them
from being dashed to pieces against a stage box.
The effect of this marine illusion is to diffuse, as
’twere, a smell of seaweed and winkles throughout
the house. Walter Baker, on his way to and fro
the gallows, with the sheriff and a few hangmen
in attendance, is another beautiful scene. As a
rule, the condemned felon doesn't walk hack from
the gallows, hut in Baker’s case the drop refuses
to work, and after the Law has made three abor-
tive attempts to drop him through the floor, he is
sent to Portland and chained to Albert Norman’s
leg. _ Cosgrove figures prominently as a fisherman
of insane habits, hut the third villain is a
feeble creature who carries a new umbrella
and serves as a target for the funny man’s
jokes. “Saved from the Sea,” Thk BULLETIN
regrets to mention, doesn’t teach Thrift to the
gallery. Every night a certain number of ap-
parently real mutton chops are grilled over a real
picnic fire and handed round, hut not one member
of the festive party has thus far been observed to
make a hearty meal, and the chops are probably
mutilated in due course by the stage cat.

Speaking in N.S. VV Log. Assembly, Labor-
leader M'Gowen remarked that the Labor-party
was there to see that Premier Reid, who is now
preaching “moderation,” did not sacrifice prin-
ciple for policy. It might be expedient and judi-
cious for the Premier to go back with regard to
the proposed Land-tax exemptions, but the Labor-
party was not going back. The Premier, tie
added, somewhat misunderstood an incident in
the Labor-party’s history. He had said that four
members of the party were rejected at the previous
elections because they voted for the Dibbs duties.
That was not so. The reason those men were
rejected from the Labor-party was that they did
not vote straight on the Broken Hill matter.

Splendid coffee is being grown in N.
Queensland; and the province is,also starting
with tea, in which, however. Natal has antici-
pated her. Last year Natal had 2000 acres of tea
under cultivation, and exported half-a-million
pounds of the leaf. Note : Coffee and tea imply
generally black and yellow labor.
