The Argus replaces its Bret Harte story,
just concluded, with a gory, sensational screamer,
by nobody in particular. It is billed in all the
news-agents with cuts of a scrimmage on a steam-
in the agonised presence of a long-haired
heroine, with a detective and the funny-man
watching on the bank. The whole suggests Bland
Holt and looks like the “Argus, with which is
incorporated ‘Deadvvood Dick.’”
“ Ishmael ” Crick, M.P, (N.S.W.), should
remember that the qualifications of a political
leader should not merely include brilliancy,
honesty of purpose, audacity, bulldog tenacity
and gadfly persistency, but also that power of
endurance which enables him to possess his soul
in patience with unshaken faith in his star,
besides that faculty of self-command which can
successfully conceal the irritation of “ a sore
head.” Without these attributes, even political
genius courts political failure.
N.S.W, Premier Reid, who swore to
"‘stick to every line of the land-tax exemptions,”
now shuffles about in search of a compromise. If
ho succeeds, he will have destroyed about his last
claim to democratic favor. His reported inten-
tion of putting the exemption clauses in italics in
the Land-tax Bill, and then making believe they
aren’t there, is so unspeakably mean in the face of
his former protestations that the Upper House
makes uo secret of its contempt for him.
Gospel religion is ludicrously inconsistent.
A female missionary goes rampaging abroad with
an inspired New Testament in her handbag, and
the blessed words of Paul buckling from her
tonguebut when some of Paul’s inspirations
don’t suit her own hook, she puts her tongue in
her cheek and swallows the unsavoury morsels.
Hear apostle P.— Ist Cor. 14th chap, verses 34
and 35, “ Let your women keep silence in the
churches; for it is not permitted unto them to
speak . . . And if they will learn anything, let
them ask their husbands at home; for it is a
shame for women to speak in the church.” Also
Ist lim. 2nd chap, verse 12. “But I suffer not a
woman to teach nor to usurp authority over the
man, but to be in silence.” Practically the female
missionary asserts that inspired Paul was a
narrow-minded bigot who didn’t know any better,
and probably she is quite right. So she preaches
the divine Gospel of Christ, who, when on earth,
loved all the uneducated believers in him, and
simply damned the more cultured listeners who
didn’t believe. The Christianity which awards
hell-fire to every man who calls’another man a
fool, merely because the Pharisees applied that
contemptuous term to the Nazarene; and which
has been cursing generations of Jew “ vipers ” for
the last 1800 years. The Christianity of the
Book ; of the Ajawtles who record their Master’s
promise to comedown from Heaven on clouds and
rule the earth during their lifetime. And unless
the pore heathen believe certain things set forth in
the Book, whilst shutting their eyes and ears and
minds to other things (viz.,the test passages by
which the falsehood of the Book’s pretensions is
revealed) they must follow their fathers to Sheol,
Paul, or no Paul, missionary teaching of that sort
is deplorable in women, as in men.
“Aby” Alfcson’s original picture, “The
Golden Age,” which, under the terms of his
travelling scholarship, he presents to the Vio.
National Gallery, lately arrived. A brilliant—
almost audacious—piece of work, it is placed in
the midst of a score of dull, minor-keyed can-
vases, now utterly useless, for nobody will
bestow even a passing glance upon them wliile
Altson’s bold “ mustard ” conception, as it was
known among his Parisian fellow-students, re-
mains. An oblong picture about Bft. x 4ft., with
a huge irregular splash of yellow over three-fourths
of the whole and adozen smaller pink splashesover
the green remainder, the work at a distance strikes
the spectator ns the chef d'oeuvre of some
artistic lunatic; but few steps forward are needed
to dispel any question of the artist’s sanity.
Five nude female figures are grouped about an
almost nude male figure. One Rossell-like nymph
stands on tip-toe, her arms extended above her
head and her hair flying in the air ; she is in the
midst of some weird, enthralling dance—the
accompaniment being provided by the youthful
flautist in the rear, and a female harpist
on the right. , All are exquisitely drawn
and painted—a trifle under-inodelh d perhaps,
especially the girl on the extreme right-hand of
the picture—and the effect of (notion is conveyed
with ten times the force of the over-rated, over-
paid “Right of Way.” But the great charm
of the picture is its imninousness; the sunlight
falling through the boughs, touching the dancing
nymph on the calf, is real sunlight; its spots on the
grass reflect real warm glows upon the side of the
squatting girl by the male musician. It is a
picture before which the frozen Melbournian can
stand for five minutes and imagine he is back
again at February’s picnics.

The Boli-etin has already drawn attention
to the farewell benefit to be given to Geo. Rignold
on 10th Sept., and it wishes to draw attention
some more. Everybody agrees that Mr. Rignold
deserves a benefit, and he proposes to earn it by
delivering the famous oration which Mr. Anthony
delivered for Mr. Csesar’s benefit; while as many
more first-class artists as can be crammed into the
programme will be employed to make a blaze
worthy of the occasion.
w J * * « *
A Sydney concert promoter announces a
new idea, which is none the less promising because
it isn’t new at all. The public, he says, are weary
of the old singers and the old songs. Give ’em
sentiment. So he is starting a series of Scotch
evenings, Irish evenings, war-song evenings, sea-
song evenings, and even Australian evenings, in
the Town Hall ; and as the talent is good, the
price of admission (Is.) is low, and arrangements
have been made for smokers, promenaders, and
refreshers, the great soul of the public ought to
catch on.

The Brough-Boucicault co. undergoes many changes
after the Melb. season. Beryl Faber is the only one of
the last hatch of importations who will stay on: she has
signed for another nine months, and will show again in
Svdney and Melb, Geraldine Olhffe and her husband, with
Mr. Elwood and his wife, the pleasant-looking Miss Talbot,
go ’Owe from Adelaide. The dainty Grace Noble will do
likewise, having put in three years’ useful service. Jenny
Watt-Tanner will take up Miss Talbot’s maternal parts.
Hilda Spong replaces Grace Noble, and Mrs. Brough
resumes her old position vice Miss Olliffe.
Hilda Spong, who Joins B. and B. immediately, has
picked up two or three trying mannerisms since her last
appearance with the premier Australian eo. It will take
Hot B. all he knows to modify the masculine strides she
now makes in her profession ; also, to break her of her
lingual peculiarities—“ It is a fine dee,” “Goo awee," &c.
Hilda is usually a heroine whom any judicious and right-
minded hero must love to gather to the spotless white
front of hjs dress-shirt, an 1 it will he a matter of grave
Australasian concern if D. Boucicault fails to abolish these
spots on the star.
After fire nights of “A Village Priest,’’ at Meih. Prin*
cess’, “Mrs. Tank." goes up again for one night only,
and admirers of both will have a good chance of compar-
ing Grundy and Pinero at close quarters, and at their
best. Mrs. Grundy appreciates the former, but he is
much weighted by ids want of humor. “ A Vill-
age Priest” is only relieved by the inclusion of the Holy
father's housekeeper, and that is not a relief deserving of
much thanks. In “Mrs. Tanqueray ” we have Cayley,
the Orreyds, and the Second Mrs. T. herself, a splendid
composition of light,and shade. In “ Sowing the Wind ”
Grundy is more successful, but the blend is not nearly so
artistic. With Pinero you never discover the soda in the
cake. ,

Dear Bulletin,— l'm a shy man from the hush, hut I
never felt more strongly tempted to make a public oration
than 1 did last night at Sydney Lyceum theatre. When
Louis Hradfield returned to give another verse of “ Her
golden teeth were hanging on a tack,” or something to
that effect, I was only restrained by fear of violence from
shouting; “Mister Bradfield, I came 400 mi leg and paid
6->. to see this show, hut the hediamonded cacklers around
me are so busy assuring each other that they’ve ‘heard
it all b foah,’ that I can’t hear a word from ttie stage,
and as I didn’t come to hear a corrohoree of ‘granny
birds,’ who are evidently moulting, I’ll retire to some
other show.” Some day a few boys from “ out back,” wdio
come to listen and lake back a few fragments of song to
their mates, will arise and make an emphatic protest
against the tiiealre-parlies who come only to hear them-
selves talk. If the present writer ever arrives at ( lie last
Judgment, lie will not he surprised to hear the Lyceum
diess-oircle crowd remarking, in loud, husky tones, that
“Haw! it’s not had, tint we’ve heard it all befoih, don-
oherknow !” But why don’t the entrepreneurs “ take
steps”?— H.H.C.C.

through England, Germany, France, and America, look-
ing belter than The Bulletin has ever s-en him look
before. In London lie produced “ Robbery Under Arms ”
for three weeks at the Princess’ with very fair success.
In New York, lie was handsomely paid for “ underwriringa
play ";in ’Frisco lie made a great hit as Valjean in “ Saints
and Sinners ”(“ Les Mis6rahles ”), and a still greater hit
as Hamlet. When in London, Dampier and George Miln
took one another to interview Irving. The great man
said nothing about coming to Australia ; hut the Kendalls
may possibly paralyse this cynical continent next year.
Melba’s singing in “ Lea Huguenots” the traveller found
superb ; but what moat kindled Ins dramatic enthusiasm
was the reverence paid to the immortal William by Ger-
many, “ (he true home of the Shakespearean spirit.” In
Berlin, Dampier saw in one week five of Shakespeare’s
comedies produced—each with perfect detail and unsur-
passable splendour. -

The Wild - Cat Column .
In Q. Supreme Court the other (lay, the legal
stuffing was knocked out of that fascinating implement
of commerce, the postdated cheque. On March 15 one
Magill gave a cheque payable on April 6. The B.N.Q.
paid it on March 19. Consequently, another clieque given
by Magill on March 16 was dishonored on March 26 for
want of the funds which the post-dated cheque had
absorbed. Magill sued before Judge Cooper and a Towns-
ville jury and got t5O damages. The hank appealed, and
Chief Justice Griffith, Judge Harding and Judge Heal all
decided in its favor. Griffith, J., held that a post-dated
cheque is a hi 11 of exchange payable on demand ; that it
should bear a Is., not a Id. stamp; that its real date is
the date of the tvsue, and the dale on the face is
uolhing. Harding, J., wasn’t sure that a postdated
cheque is a hill of exchange payable on demand ;
but held that if it is presented for payment, and a hank
lias funds to meet it, it cannot refuse to accept the
responsibility of even a future piynvnt, and that when it
accepts the responsibility it is justified in charging (lie
clieque against the drawer’s account straight away. And
Judge Real agreed both with Judge llarding ai d the
Onief Justice.
The lay mind can only marvel at this logic. The
establishment of a. new hanking convention does not very
much matter, so long as it is generally understood. What
the decision amounts to, however, is this. If John Smith
authorises ids hanker in black and white to pay Tom
Jones £6 on f >o c. 1, '96, and hands over the authority to
Jones on S ,»u 1, ’95, it is the bank's privilege, even
its duty, to pay Jones £5 as soon as he likes to
present the cheque. Why, in the name of common
sense an instrument which plainly declares that it isn’t
negotiable before (lie date on its face, should he legally
made negotiable three months before that dale doesn’t
anywhere appear. The common-sense view is that a
post-dated clieque has no negotiable value whatever till
its date arrives. Its authority lies dormant till it
matures, just like that of a promissory-note ; and pend-
ing maturity it lias exactly I lie same commercial value ns
a promissory-note— i.e., it is a promise to pay on a given
date, which may or may not be worthless, and which is
only accepted as a provisional settlement of accounts.
But no bank would venture to hypothecate its client's
funds to pay his promissory-notes before they matured.
And why thelaw. in the case of a post-dated cheque, in-
sists on construing an authority to pay money on a
specified date as an authority to pay it tv fore that, dateis
a puzzle to which even the alleged wisdom of the law can
supply no sensible solution.
♦ • * •
Tucker, late chairman of Vic. Tariff Board
deceased, makes three proposals towards freeing Vie.
ports. Tonnage dues (which bring in £lB,oooper annum)
are to he abolished. Pilotage charges, comprising the
present earnings of the pilots, are to he reduced one-
third. Thirdly, vessels entering in ballast ate to lie
charged only half pilotage dues. The pilotage charges
amount to about £5OO per annum for 21 pilots—say,
£ll,OOO every year. It would not lie a grave matter to
take over the pilots and make them Government officials,
and then give their services free to every vessel that
wants them. The tonnage dues are merely lolls, and
tolls on roads hav e long been abandoned by every civilised
community.
Vic. at present is indulging an export policy,
and as long as that is consirt red desirable and advan-
tageous, no hindrance to exportation should be permitted
to exist. Wharfage dues and tonnage dues are a survival
of barbarism—an undesirable impost on desirable trade.
Vie. already penalises certain import* in the interests of
local production. At present Melh. is nn expensive har-
borage—a condition of things favorable to the shipping
ring which is cynically dictating terms to the Vic. Govt,
The alleged “ revenue ” from tonnage dues is in reality
expenditure by the community on a cumbrous system.

One of the champion mean men is a fellow who,
in business, frequently sells a line amounting to £2.
According to law, lie should put a penny-stamp on re-
ceipts for £2 and over. So he just makes out an account
for £ll9s. Hid., takes £2, and gives the ha’penny change
thus saving a ha’penny.

Bendigo (Vic.) Co-operative Bakery, at its
eighth half-yearly meeting, declared a div. of a farthing
in the 41b. loaf. The co., since it started three and a-half
years ago, has made a profit of £lOl3 on a capital of £562,
whilst its premises, paid for, are worth, even at present
values, more than its paid-up capital.

Wheat-growing in Victoria is experiencing a

will pay. That the cultivation of thousands of acres of

capital will quickly tie diverted to land. Of course, it will

An English chemist is said to have discovered a
means of increasing the illuminating power of gas
thirteenfold. Experiments with that result were lately
made at Greenwich Observatory, and the electric-light
companies show some alarm.
Judge Hodges, summing up the Customs frauds
(Melh.) case against. IlosenMd and Co., echoed the un-
answerable argument that old Judge Molesworth
used years ago, when a soft-goods firm was denying
similar swindles in the same court. If the charge is
false, why not produce the hooka and disprove it ? At
the present moment several highly respectable merchants
are making frantic efforts to get their hooks into such a
state of confusion thatlhej mightchance producing them
“at a pinch.”

One of the Freetrade industries of N.S.W. is
the manufacture of high-class rum, from 60 to 800. p.,
for export to IJingland. The correlative Hinglish indus-
try is to dilute, adulterate, bottle, and export that rum
to N.S.W. as “best Jamaica.” It would never do f.-r
N.S.W. rum distillers to manufacture for the N.S.W.
market , because the excise duly is as heavy as tlie import-
duty. Besides, the present system fattens the bloated
ring of right-thinking Sydney importers, encourages the
potato spirit “scab*’ distiller, and poisons off those ob-
jectionable working-men who patronise, the Accursed
Drink at “ thrippenoe a nobbier.”

The B.H. Proprietary directors, having got

not such an expensive fire after all. Chairman M’Bryde,

would he under 420,000. The damage— accurate estimate

The employes of the B.H. Proprietary Co., of
course, had nothing to do with bringing about the late
change of management (writes a correspondent), but
Otherwise the position at tbe mine lends itself to compari-
son with that, pictured in the faille of the frogs and their
kings. Manager Howell was never p'pnlar, hut most of
the employes in the 00. would be delighted could they
have King Stork deposed with great violence, and get
King Log hack again.
At latest they believed they bad smothered the
fire in the Broken’ Hill mine, 'tint were still forcing carbonic
acid gas down into the workings. Broken Hill was
greatly alarmed about the introduction of this gas ; hut,
after all, it was very much diluted, three-fourths of the
whole volume being atmospheric air. The Southern nest
of furnaces on the mines has not been blown in again,
liow r ever; and it is very likely these w ill not be used much
more. Altogether, in fact, unless something quite out of
the way turns up. the days of much smelting at Broken
Hill are quite numbered. Mr. Howell never doubted for
a moment that the sulphides would have to he taken
to the seaboard: and Professor Schnabel dismissed fire-
treatment on the mine as ntisnid. The battle will be
between S.A. and N.S.W. sea-board, with appearances at
present all in favor of the latter.

on £140,000 for five-sixths of the mine. Why 7 Because of
Its really-proven value? No. Because they all knew that,
with suoli a gigantically-advertised •ensation, there were
tools enough in London to give |hem more promotion
profit on a risk of £lO,OOO than tlie owners got for the
mine. The Londonderry's ** face " was its fortune.

ounces of 37 tons of gold, worth £5,000,000 sterling ; and
has paid £3,500,000 in divs. And there are many more
tons to follow.

Mills' United and to several of the big claims piling up
big reserves: the Brilliant Block, Brilliant and St.

have put by £20,000 in cash. The Brilliant Block is
starting to erect a big battery, and Kelly’s Block needs
its reserve for its straight shaft; the George director*
are of the opinion so big a property should have a .thump-
ing cash reserve. Brilliant and St. George for the month
gave 2323 tons for 42900 z. ; div., £5400. The Brilliant,

Dark River Pyrites Works, completed at a cost
of over £3OOO, after t reating about 100 tons of ore, clo-ed
dewn, the reason assigned being the want of stuff for
treatment. The same fate has attended thejOmeo works
and those at Glen Wills, which shows the folly of rushing
into expensive machinery in places difficult of access until
the mines have been properly developed. An effort is
being made to induce tire leaseholders to co-operate with
the Dark lliver Pyrites co. in forming a new co.—a course
which should have been adopted at the outset.
• • • •
Probably no mining scrip on earth has fluctuated
so regularly’ and methodically as Sulieman Pasha’s, Bal-
larat. The old down-East mine is a pretty consistent per-
former, and has turned out a lot more gold than many of
the sensational claims, hut the scrip has rhythmically
see-sawed for the last 15 years. During that time any
man might have lived in luxury by regularly buying in
the fall and selling on the sure and certain rise of this
stock alone. Shares are now on the up-grade. There are
a few old "corner pien ” in Ballarat who have had the
swing of the (Ling for a long time now.
The portrait of footballer Abbott in The
Bulletin of 10th Aug. was from an excellent
photo, by Freeman and Co., of Sydney.
Mr. Illingworth, sculptor, of Sydney, has
recently modelled two excellent medallions of
Parkes and Premier Reid. Both are effective
portraits—that of the shaggy ex-Preinier being
especially artistic.
Mrs. Butler, the now fully - vindicated
victim of the recent shameful N.S.W. divorce*
conspiracy, wishes it to be known, with reference
to certain statements in the press, that, strongly
believing in her innocence and pitying her
destitute condition, Mr. it. Cooke, private-enquiry
agent, of 133 King-st., Sydney, took up her case,
disentangled the web of the conspiracy, and pro*
cured the conviction of the criminals, without the
prospect of gaining a single shilling by his
exertions.
“Still Apart,” song, by Dr. MacCarthy, as
sung by Mine. Enriquez, has won a pronounced
success.*
The Ivatoomba Waltz, by M. M. Maguire,
is the ballroom favorite at Government House
and elsewhere. Its pleasing melodies are an im-
portant item in all programmes of dance and
popular music.*
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating' properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now, a beautiful complexion bf
peach and ivory mav be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

Personal Items.
J. C. Williamson will be 51 on 26th Aug.
, Speaker Graham Berry will be 73 on
August 28.
The incoming N.S. W. Governor is an
almost absolutely bald-headed man.
Agent-General Play ford’s knighthood is
getting nearer. He now suffers from gout.
A Westralian who has successfully floated
several wild-cats is known as Hannan-ias.
The late Martin Loughlin seems to have
been boss partner in the City of Melb. Bank, with
7175 shares.
The once-famous “Boy-politician” Caul-
field was, at latest, in Melbourne hospital in a
very serious condition of health.
Those in the know aver that the last chap-
ter of Deeming’s autobiography will make pretty
reading when its time arrives.
A firm of London publishers will bring out
shortly a volume of Randolph Bedford’s short
stories, reprinted from the Argus, Bulletin, and
other papers.
Hartley Williams was the Victorian judi-
cial and, indeed, legal pioneer of the bicycle, and
nearly ten years ago scandalised the particular by
wt whizzing from Castlemaine to Bendigo.
Photo, by Falk.
The Late J. P. MACDONALD (late
Manacjer Her Majesty's. Theaire).
Louis Ellis, Yic, ex-sheriff, says that when
he was at school in Hyde Park, Sydney, 40 years
ago, he only “played the wag” once, and that
was, to see a mau hanged. The boy is father
of the man.
Two members of the Turner (Vic.) Min-
istry, Peacock and Isaacs, have been State school-
teachers, Peacock’s success as Minister of Educa-
tion springs from this intimate knowledge of
State school work.
Turves, in his causerie to the sucking
lawyers, belled the cat about Higinbotham. He
showed that this great man’s forte was not the
judicial, wherein, indeed, he became of strangely-
exaggerated punctilio for sucli a democrat.
Dr. F. T. W. Ford, an old medico who
started (notwithstanding his M.D. degree) in
Victoria as a digger, and was subsequently for
many years police-surgeon at Melb., lives now in
quiet retirement at Cheltenham (Eng.).
Annear, in Bananaland Parliament;
“Yes, I would give property a vote. If I ’ave a
’ouse, why shouldn’t I ’ave a voice in the ’ouse? ’’
There are many things a man ought to ’ave in a
’ouse which he ’asn’t. For instance, the letter
Ne Sutor : If Dr. O’Hara wouldn’t go
singing “Elijahs,” and fighting Purveses, he
would be elected at Melb, hospital. Tire dull
public don’t believe in Admirable Crichtons, even
when their election-bills ace backed by an Acting-

The monument erected in Melb. cemetery
to the memory of Tommy Corrigan, the famous
steeplechase jockey, by his wife, is in good taste ;
and the grave is turfed over apropos of the quota-
tion “ O i and beneath the turf all men are equal,”
inscribed above.
Dr- Herman Lawrence, the one “new '
man who got elected to the Melbourne Hospita*
staff, was the hero of the first wedding perpetrated
in St. Paul’s (Melb.) Cathedral. Or rather, he
was the heroine's bridegroom. There is no such
thing as a hero at a wedding.
It gives one a nasty shock to find the ghost
of Harrie Smith standing outside the Melbourne
Athenaeum Club, looking for all the world like the
deceased sport revisiting his old haunts. The
apparition is Harrie’s brother. Such family like-
nesses ought not to be allowed.
At the N.S. W. Leg. Council opening Dr.
Garran, one of the three commissioners, wore the
conventional dress-suit, but President Lackey and
Attorney-General “Jack” Want appeared in
knee-breeches, buckles and ruffles. Just think of
it! Jack Want in ruffles !
Barrister Purves’ lecture at Melbourne
Athenseum on “ Bench and Bar ” was, in parts,
of a strong indigo tint. As the ages of the
majority of his auditors ranged from 16 to 23,
their mothers consider that the Q.C. might have
toned down his anecdotes considerably.
The great strength outside Parliament of
Symon, Q.C., leader of the S.A. Conservatives, is
that not a whisper has ever been breathed against
hia personal character. Yet he is not popular, for
there is great faith in the doctrine that “ to err is
human,” and that moral perfection is abnormal.
Ex-Premier Patterson, apropos of the
totalisator-legislation question, warmly denounces
the idea of the State “ recognising vice in any
form,” Of.course, it isn’t to the point that the
State has made J. B. Patterson a knight ?
N.S. W. Colonial-Secretary Brunker (at
Adamstown public-school): “My dear boys, I
have nevah yet attempted to smoke, nor to drink
a glass of grog; and I am now, by God’s blessing,
Chief Secretary of Noo South Wales.” The boys
afterwards : ‘‘Hi; let’s play Molly Brunker ! ”
George Coppin, Melb. Theatre Royal,
retires from the Leg. Council next week. George
is 75 years of age, and first entered the Council in
1858. Looking at the old man now it is difficult
to believe that, as a young man, he played Cupid
perfectly at Drury Lane, and was considered to
look the part to perfection,
Monahi Iri, who defended the Kaiapoi
pah against Te Rauparaha, when the latter gentle
assimilator of his race made his memorable in-
vasion of the Middle island of Maoriland, in or
about 1830, died only the other day. Though for
the last 40 years of his life he lived surrounded by
Europeans, he never learned a word of English.
Justice Richmond, of Maoriland Supreme
Court, is dead. He it was who presided at the
memorable trial of sea-captain Jarvie for alleged
poisoning. Ireland, Q.C., was specially brought
from Victoria to defend, and Dr. Macadam, the
Vic. Govt, analyst, gave professional evidence in
the case. The prisoner was convicted and hanged.
The death is recorded, at the age of 82,
of Mrs. Plunkett, relict of John Hubert Plunkett,
Imperial Attorney-General in the days of the
“System,” and in the era of responsible govern-
ment President of N.S.W. Legislative Council.
Mr. Plunkett first came to Australia with a letter
of introduction from Daniel O’Connell.
The election of R. D. Meagher, “Pos-
sum ” Dean’s solicitor, for the Phillip division of
Sydney, recalls the election to the Commons of
Dr. Kenealy, Q.C., Orton’s counsel, while the
claimant’s trial was in progress. Public sympathy
was at that time with the Wagga Wagga butcher
as it was always with the North Shore Ferry
captain.
Knights James Frederick Palmer and
StawelJ were the first Victorians to be thus
labelled. Palmer, President of the Leg. Council,
though a qualified doctor, at one time hawked
ginger-beer in Melb. When he died, Coppin
bought his hilly estate by the Yarra, called it St.
James’s Park, after him, and made a heap of
money by cutting it up.
Clever, honest Nat. Levi is one of the un-
luekiest Melbournians, for he has wrought up two
grand ideas from which inferior men will reap the
cash—as with M‘Coll, the canal projector. Nat.’s
two cards, which ought to have been trumps to
him, were Victorian coal and beet sugar. Now
Nat keeps whittling round his No. 3 idea, the
Melb. Evening News.
Whenever the subject of longevity is being
discussed, the veteran journalist, David Blair,
always tells that he was born in 1820, and that his
grandfather, who first saw the light in 1750, did
not die until he (D. B.) was a young man of 17
years. His grandfather, when a child, had seen
and spoken to very old men, who fought at the
Battle of the Boyne in 16S!).

Vic. Govt. Geologist
Murray has applied for his
pension, and been refused.
Victoria is quite right, for
Mr. Murray being one of
the best of all Australian
geologists, and in the very
prime of life, his superan-
nuation would be a public
calamity. Many stories are
told of the scientist with
the peculiar halt in his
speech. Some years ago
R. T. Vale, one of the
members for Ballarat, who
was “ great ” on the
mineral resources of the
province, walked into Muri.
ray’s office with a brown-
paper parcel which he
unfolded on the table, d s-
playing a large lump of
potato - colored “stuff."
Then, striking an attitude,
“ There, Mr. Murray,
what do you think of that,
sir?” “Wh- wh - what’s
that? ’’ asked the geologist.
“Coal, sir, Skipton coal,
and I would like your
candid opinion of it,” said
Vale, triumphantly. Mur-
ray bent over it for a few
seconds, then asked : “ Mr.
Vale, do you b-b-believe in
the eventual destruction of
all life b-b-by fire ?” “Cer-
tainly, sir.” “ Then,” con-
tinued the scholar in
minerals, “my b-b-best
advice is : when you think
that t-t-time is near at
hand you d-d-dig a hole in.
this st-t-tuff and p-p-plant
yourself there. You’ll
n-n-never get burnt.”
Kindly letter received by Capt. Fawkes,
one of the survivors of the wrecked Catterthun :
In writing my congratulations on your narrow escape,
sundry ideas are suggested, such as “old Nick takes care
of his own,” or Providence has decreed that you are to
be hanged. Anyhow, I’m right glad you are still a factor
incur little world. One of your friends declared in my
hearing, when we were discussing your escape, that
“ Fawkes would get out of anything, Darlinghurst in-
cluded.” Hoping your health is Al, I am, etc., .

for the first time. “ I took Her Majesty just as I
wad anny ither pairson ; and when I’d settled her
I said : ‘ Wad it please Yer Majesty to put on a
more favorable countenance ! ’ And she said,
‘Sairtinly, Mr. Dooney.’”

The noble name of Jones threw its baleful
shadow over a recent Melb. (Vic.) shipping-
inquest. He who fell down the hatchway and
necessitated the proceedings was Jones. The
captain of the vessel was also Jones (no relation to
No. 1). The first mate answered to Jones (no
relation to either of the foregoing). Then the
constable who removed the corpse arose and
solemnly assured the court that he likewise was
Jones. And lastly it transpired that the under-
taker’s name was Jones.
A plucky pioneer has just passed over at
86 years, at Charters Towers —John Leahy, who
paid down £4O “for his passage to Port Phillip
in 1839,” the ship being the Duke of Argyle,
whereby also came as passenger John O’Shanassy,
afterwards Premier of the province. It was Leahy
who “backed” Esmond, the gold-finder, who
came from California with Hargreaves, while the
latter searched Buninyong. Leahy also put in
an appearance at Eureka Stockade in Dec., 1854,
and had resided with his son at Charters Towers
for 20 years before his death.
NOT TO BE HAD.
BUSHMAN (who has just woke up after a big drunk) : “ Who are
YOU ?”
LANDLORD OF SHANTY: “ I’m the landlord.”
BUSHMAN: “ Well, you can’t kid me that I was as drunk as all
that last night.”
Gurr, M.P. for Geelong, is the baldest man
in Vic. Assembly, and glories in his affliction.
Life, he says, is too short for a man to be pestered
with the care of a head of hair. On one occasion,
when addressing his constituents, be told an
anecdote founded on a moving experience of his
own, concluding : “ And, gentlemen, I assure you
I escaped death by a hair’s breadth.” It was his
best anecdote, and he paused after its delivery
for the cheers. But an icy silence reigned for a
few moments, and was broken by a shrill voice
near the door : “ Say, mister, where did you get
that hair ? ”
There are three Israelites in the new
N.S.W. Parliament — viz., Collins, of Narrabri;
Phillips, of Dubbo; and (whisper it gently) Harry
Levien. Of these the last-named is an Opposi-
tionist. the middle man a Govt, supporter, and
the first a cross-bench “road-and-bridge” member.
They are thus distributed about the Chamber,
making a perfect political Mosaic, so to speak.

N.S.W. Premier Reid is a barrister,
though not exactly a star barrister. One of his
most fervid supporters, addressing a city meet-
ing in opposition to B. R. Wise during the elec-
tion, exclaimed : “You can never trust those fel-
lows who wear horsehair,” A shout of laughter
went up as the speaker was reminded that Premier
Reid occasionally wore horsehair. “Ah, yes,”
replied the partisan of Georgey Porgey, “but
only occasionally.” The answer fitted the bill.
A well-known classical tutor, who has
coached hundreds of young Victorians for the‘
University, remembers the ill-fated Saunders;
girls, slaughtered in China, as the most likeable,
lively, and troublesome of all his pupils, past or
present. Those were their frisky days, however,
before the advent of evangelist Clarke. After-
wards they sobered down, renouncing dancing
and kindred frivolities, to throw themselves with
characteristic “go ” and energy into mission pro-
jects.
Col. “ Fire-low ” Price, so goes the story,
was once imprisoned in Pentridge. Forty years
ago, when his father, the doughty John Price,-
was Inspector-General of Vic. Penal Establish-
ments, “Tom” (as the Col. was christened) in--
! irred the old man’s wrath through some boyish'
eioapade, and was ordered into the garden of
the governor’s house for three weeks’ hard,
labor. There was an armed warder hard by,
“ See that this young man keeps to his work and
goes to his meals and knocks off when the othn
prisoners are going,” said the genial Price, pere,
“Tom” did his time like a man. Had he at
tempted to escape, the warder would have hac'
to fire very low.
Henniker Heaton (whose son, late of
Eton, has just passed his exam, for the Guards
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent) h
just now much chagrined. He fully expected tin
Postmaster-Generalship, and they do say he sal
at home for four days awaiting a message from
Salisbury—but, alas, none arrived. That there
is some color of truth in this story is evidenced
by the fact that numbers of printed copies of
press-opinions with regard to his fitness for the
office have been circulated broadcast. If Henniker
himself caused this to be done, it was a fatal error
of judgment; and if it was the work of his friends,
they have done him an irreparable injury. Self-
advertising is an unpardonable sin in the eyes of
the Tory leaders, and seldom do those wl o
practise it to any extent ever get anything for
their pains. But Heaton, if he really expectedl
ever to be made Postmaster-General, is a muclr
deluded man. He is too vigorous and persistent
a reformer, and would only have been a disap-
pointment ; for, once in office, he would never
have dared to introduce any of the great changes
he has pegged at for the last six or seven years,
and, as for penny ocean postage, no Chancellor of
the Exchequer would ever consent to the loss of
revenue it would entail.

Don’t imagine for a moment that happiness
depends upon surroundings. It is more often the
result of disposition.
Don’t deceive yourself by thinking you can
succeed or be happy with a bad disposition. You
cannot.
Don’t make the mistake of thinking you can
have an amiable disposition if you have bad health,
or if there is anything deranged about your system.
Don’t misunderstand the cause of bad feelings,
irregular pains, weaknesses and depressions.
They all have a common cause.
Don’t doubt what so many eminent personages
have declared, that there is one great cause for
most of the troubles of men and women—deranged
kidneys.
Don’t deceive yourself by allowing these things
to run along. Take them in time and by the best
and most scientific manner possible.
Don’t let anyone deceive you. There is only
one great remedy known to the world for the cure
of kidney and liver troubles. That remedy is
Warner’s Safe Cure.

so long'as you feel any of the troubles above des-
cribed. It is certain to help you and prove “a
friend in need.”*

POLITICAL
POINTS
Premier Reid’s appointments have in-
creased the number of lawyers in N.S.W. Leg.
Council to 15.
At the recent Auckland Parliamentary
bye-electicn the women polled G to 4 of the
total 10,000 votes recorded.
N.S.W. Legislative Council now consists
exactly of 70 members. Appointed by Cowper, 1;
Robertson, 1; Stuart, 8; Reid, 12; Dibbs, 15;
Parkes, 33.
As the present N.S.W. Parliamentary
session is to be chiefly a financial one, Premier
Reid ought to bring in a bill to prove that two-
and-two make five.
In June, Australia paid £2625 for the use
of six English Governors. Yet only one of those
Governors was then in Australia. Perhaps that
is why Heaven sends no rain.
Murray’s action, in refraining from going
on with the division on the Yic. Totalisator Bill,
saved several members from a showing-up. They
would have found it hard to account for a glaring
change of front.
Brief, clear-cut bit of Tasmanian legisla-
tion : “ From and after the passing of this Act no
order for the attachment of any of the wages of
any servant, laborer, or workman, shall be made
by the Judge of any court.”
Sydney Daily Telegraph unabashedly
undertakes the task of teaching Premier Reid
how he can have absolute Freetrade and at the
same time execute reciprocal Customs treaties
between N.S. Wales and other countries !
Several of the prominent S. A. stone wallers,
notably Ash and Castine, are under bond with
Premier Kingston not to talk unnecessarily this
session, the object-being to prorogue next Nov. in
view of the 1896 elections, which promise to be
very stormy.
N.S.W. Opposition chief Lyne’s touching
reference to his late “revered leader” caused
someone to suggest that Lyne should resign in
favor of Dibbs. “You had better resign your-
self,” said Lyne. “ But I don’t want to see him
back,” retorted the other.
Tas. Assembly has agreed to give women a
vote as property-owners and occupiers, both for
Council and Assembly, under the new Constitu-
tion Act. Tasmania is such an obscure little place
that this new triumph of “ the cause ” has passed
almost unnoticed on the mainland.
Sydney Municipal Council has now but
one representative (the much-gilded Matt. Harris)
in N.S.W. Leg. Assembly. Time was when it
had nearly half-a-score; and now, for the first
time in its history, the N.S.W. Leg. Council
numbers two Sydney aldermen within its fold.
N.S.W. Premier Reid (on the Piddington-
Dibbs scrum at Tamworth) : “David was a match
for Goliath in that place.” Yes, and the clown
Plunderus was a match for the Giant Blunderbore
in King Division (Sydney). But these be merely
stray hurricane flotsams in the big sea of history.
A political resurrection of fatal importance
(to the N.S.W. Hansard staff) is Mr. Jawbone
Neild. This is a gentleman who once spoke for
nine hours consecutively. At least the hours were
consecutive; the speech wasn’t. Since this out-
rage occurred, the standing orders have been
altered accordingly.

N.S.W. EX - PREMIER PARKES HAND.
Angus Cameron, M.P. (N.S.W.), is a
living example in politics of the proverb “ While
there’s life there’s hope.” Who would have ex-
pected that this able man, who had so many more
chances, politically, than other men (and missed
them) would ever have risen again, on the ashes
of his dead self, to the dignity of a very long black
coat, -a very shiny top-hat, and a tree railway-
pass?
While felicitating Angus Cameron, M.P.
(N.S.W.), on his legislative resurrection, The
Bulletin is glad to notice that he has not been
made Chairman of Committees at .£BOO a year.
Having had the front to stand as an opponent of
payment to members, Mr. Cameron is very pro-
perly allowed, so far as office is concerned, to soak
in his own gravy.
“Jim” Hogue, back again to N.S.W.
Assembly, which he first entered in charge of the
Sergeant-at-Arms, shows how the scholarly attri-
butes necessary for the editorship of Sydney E.
News combine to disqualify a man for distinction
in a deliberative Assembly. To what base uses
we may return ! But, then, the bellman at Bunny
Corner is a B. A.
Premier Reid sneaked “the Divine gui-
dance ” into the vice-regal speech at last week’s
opening of N.S.W. Parliament. The re-appear-
ance of this abandoned splinter of hypocrisy is
due to the “nonconformist conscience” of Cook,
Garrard, Gould and Co. If hon. members have
faith in “the Divine guidance,” therl let them
abolish the Sergeant-at-Arms.
Seeing that N.S.W. Leg. Council loudly
professes a burning desire to give effect to the
will of the N.S.W. people, but excuses itself from
knowing, or attempting to define, what that will
is, because of the confusion of issues in which
every Parliamentary election is clouded, now is
the time for the Assembly to send along a Plebi-
scitum and Referendum Bill. Yet the Council
pre-indicates its opposition to that form of ascer-
taining the public pleasure.
It w T as a sectional political necessity for
the N.S.W. “Reid party” to repudiate B. R.
Wise and to manoeuvre him out of his Parlia-
mentary seat. A man who so clearly saw, as did
Wise, the whole game of humbug being played by
Reid, and who did not hesitate to depict it in
naked te/ms in the letter to Parkes, written as far
back as June 4, but only printed week before last,
was too dangerous or candid a friend to hug to a
conjuror’s bosom. Wise, in the midst of the
Reid crowd, was a hissing shell with the slow-fuse
alight ; and Reid’s powder-monkeys hove him
overboard.
The long-lost Local Government Bill
doesn’t appear on the Reid (N.S. W.) Govern-
ment’s programme this session, and it seems to
have again dropped out of sight indefinitely, ihe
reason for its absence, said Premier Reid to ex-
Treas. See in reply to a question by the latter,
“is because you cut its throat.” Somebody
should take Reid aside and yell in his ear
the real facts of the ease, which are that he
deliberately withdrew the Local Government Bill
against the wishes of the majority of the House,
because the House would not let him insert into
it an altogether new proviso giving Eat Man a
maximum of 20 votes, instead of five, at munici-
pal elections—a proviso of which nobody had any
previous notice. G. Reid s Local Government
Bill wasn’t murdered ; it committed suicide.
Editor Bulletin,— The Junee Labor
League supported Arthur Rae because he is in
favor of the reforms set out in the Labor platform,
and not, as you wrongly state, because of his Free-
trade leanings. Had the fiscal curse assumed
Protectionist shape in him we would have closed
our eyes to the defect and voted for him just the
same. Also, no member of this League is
pecuniarily interested in the retention of the
grain-duties, a fact which should have a flatten-
ing-out effect on your jibe. The successful Protec-
tionist candidate here is opposed to land taxation,
also to Upper House reform, fails to see any
points in Woman Suffrage, and don’t give a rap
for the Referendum. The Bulletin may have
observed that most Protectionist patriots are
built that way.— Leaguer. [ls The Bulletin,
then, to understand that the Junee Labor League
ia in no way interested in agriculture, and that
its members demand the abolition of the produce
duties? —Ed. B.]
London cablegram ; “Great Britain will
require 192,000,000 bushels of wheat prior to Sep-
tember, ’O6. This is twice the quantity America
will be able to export to all countries.” Could
there be a much more significant commentary on
the absolute insanity of Britain’s Freetrade policy
amid a nest of warlike nations whose hostility if f
always “on the hair trigger?” England’sgranary
should be England. Given a three-weeks’
blockade of Britain, and its starving millions would
turn cannibal, and eat up the monarchy and the
lords and the plutocrats, as a first course. And
then ? Freetrade-and the idolatrously-wor-
shipped “cheap loaf” have frozen out Britain’s
food resources, and, in the day of adversity, the
harvest will be the fatted flesh of the dogmatic
and domineering Cobdenites. The boasted navy
that formerly swept the seas is the one crutch
with which the old Freetrade cripple can attempt
to defend itself. A perilous chance.

.£350,000, or a total of ,£1,250,000.

From various little signs, it is easy to see
that the S.M. Herald has no particular love for
Premier Reid—who lately addressed an election
crowd from the Daily Telegraph office-windows.
But the Herald is sleuth-like and politic. It
is not going to publicly bite out Reid’s larynx ;
but it will “ get there” by privately putting a little
septic matter on the lancet wherewith it bene-
volently opens the fat gentleman’s political boils.
An opportune argument in favor of the
“tote.” A bookmaker the other day refused to
pay a debt which he had lost, walloped the
winner—an old man—until he was dragged off
him by some bystanders, used an immense quan-
tity of bad language, then got on a horse and
chased the ancient backer and walloped him a
second time, until he was again dragged off by
some more bystanders, and yet escaped before a
spineless magistrate with a miserable fine. Also,
the bet still remains unpaid. The tote, on the
other hand, never refuses to pay its honest debts,
never swears or commits brutal assaults, never
drinks or smokes, doesn’t solicit custom or adver-
tise for it, doesn’t bribe the police or detectives,
never plays fraudulent card games, doesn’t haunt
billiard-tables or public-houses, never misconducts
itself with the pretty girl at the hotel when it has
had a good day, and doesn’t tell indecent stories,
or wear gaudy clothes and glaring red ties with big
diamonds in them, or decorate two dirty fat hands
with cameo rings. If these aren’t reasons enough
in its favor the church is hard to satisfy.
Sydney Telegraph, in a recent leader,
declares against the adoption of the minimum
wage system on Government works, on the ground
that it is contrary to the eternal doctrines of
Freeraid :
A moment’s consideration shows that if men engaged
on contracts or otherwise in work subject to Govern-
mental control, are paid a higher rate than their services
could be purchased for, they are made pensioners to the
extent of the difference upon public bounty. They stand
in exactly the same relationship to the community at
large as do the proprietors of protected industries whom
the public are compelled to subsidise in order that their
callings may be made sufficiently profitable. It is singular
to find the Minister for Works of the most thorough free-
trade Government this colony has ever had adopting the
methods of Protectionism and its logical complement,
State socialism, as in this matter he has done.
Very singular, certainly; but, then, where is the
Freetrader who dares to live up to his own soulless
creed? Even the Telegraph hasn’t begun to
demand the abolition of the N.S.W. free-news-
paper-postage system, by which it is protected,
at a heavy expense to the nation, against the com-
petition of its foreign and country contemporaries.

into the whole case, and report upon it without
delay. An able and fearless report is assured.”
In Vic. Assembly, it was sought to class
as “ urgent” the bill to provide for more popular
hours of polling, in order that the measure might
be applied to the local municipal elections on
22nd Aug. Graham Berry gave this memorable
ruling : that “ as the bill enlarged the franchise ot
the people, it was a matter of urgency.”
One thing is be-
coming tolerably cer-
tain —Victoria cannot
retrench very much
further. Premier Tur-
ner proposes to run the
province next year on a
total outlay of <£s 10s.
per head the same
rate as in 1877. But as
the interest on the
public debt is about
twice as much as it was
then, and the railway
system is immensely
larger in proportion to
population, and conse-
quently absorbs more in
wages, this really means
a very great reduction
in all other depart-
ments compared to the
estimates of 18 years
ago. If the latest re-
trenchments don’t keep
the finances square, the
time will not be very
far off when more taxa-
tion will be inevitable.
Victoria levies the
smallest Customs re-
venue per head of all
the Australasian pro-
vinces, and has also
the smallest expendi-
ture, Tasmania only
excepted, so that if
things grow much worse
the land-tax question
will come to the front
again to a certainty.
Dalrymple, chief
opponent of State
Socialism in Queens-
land, is, by the irony
of fate, the head of its
most important State
Socialistic department
viz., Education. The
eloquent Dal. wrote an
article the other day
in the Q. Educational
Journal, containing
an account of a Social-
istic experiment in
China which proved a
lamentable failure. At the end of Dalrymple s
Ministerial career he will probably be anxious to
show that State Socialism, as evidenced in the
management of the Education Department under
his supervision, has proved a magnificent success.

The Argus squeals when you touch the
shipping interests and shipping rings. Tucker’s
Free Port proposals will incidentally favor tramp-
vessels—the sweaters of the shipping system. The
encouragement of tramp ships would mean the
discouragement of shipping rings. The ARGUS :
The only weak proposal is that relating to “tramp”
vessels, which are popularly supposed to go “on spec.’
from port to port in search of cargo, like so many wander-
ing flying Dutchmen, Perhaps no great harm will he
done in making a present of a few pounds to the owners
of every “tramp” that turns up in Port Phillip, but to
make the present will be altogether a work of superero-
gation.
Yic. export trade is at present seriously threatened
by the big shipping ring. Four or five “ tramps,”
cheaply berthed in Hobson’s Bay, would bring a
change o’er the spirit of the night-mare.

WEST MAITLAND, Thursday. .
Mr. Frank Blaxland, of the firm of Higgins,
Marshall, and Co., had a miraculous escape from
terrible injuries to-day. He was classifying a
mob of cattle at Campbell's Hillsaleyards when an
infuriated bullock turned and charged madly at
the horse and rider. Being in a confined space
Mr. Blaxland was unahle to avoid the onrush.
The bullock’s horn entered the horse’s chest, in-
flicting a terrible gash, and the injured horse fell
to the ground. Mr. Blaxland extricated himself
with, great nreseneo of mind, and utilising the
bodies of the other bullock* as a guard he got
away safe.’ The bullock was subsequently driven
to the tracking yards, where the beast charged
the drover, who e dec ted a rapid exit from the
yard The bullock ■jumped a fence 7ft. high, and
continued his mad 'career after the drover, who
I was compelled to race his horse a distance of
Leveral miles in Ins effort to shake off his p-arsne*.
1 The bullock is Still at large.

The rush of debutantes to be stuck on the
sacred dais at Mrs. Madden’s acting-vice-regal
“hop” is a screaming farce. Some of them are
very downy chickens, who have been “out,” as
far as the Melb. Block is concerned, for several
seasons. They wink their other eye, the innocents,
as they go through the dais rites. The dais afore-
said is known down South as “Mrs. Madden’s
Slave-Market.”
* * #
The patronage of acting-vice-royalty didn’t
go for much at the Melb. Hospital elections last
week. Madden’s candidate, the bould O’Hara,
was beaten out of a place as indoor-surgeon by 800
votes. This deepens The Bulletin’s suspicions
re the blighting influence of His Gracious
Majesty, John M. And the more he waxes, the
more it wanes.

plastered over with :

One other Melbourne medical man, at
least, goes from the beaten track of professional
etiquette. Noticing in a ’bus, the other day, a
lady who was evidently about to increase her
worldly responsibilities, he politely presented his
card, and apologetically expressed a hope that, as
she would shortly be requiring a doctor, he would
not be forgotten. Solemn fact.
• • *
Lieut -Gov. Way has given another break-
fast to a score of assorted parsons at Adelaide
Government House. The atmosphere is gradually
being got into a fit state for the reception of Rev.
Fowell Buxton. The only thing the evangelical
brewer can now do to rehabilitate his character in
Adelaide is to utter an audible “Damn !” before
some considerable concourse of people just after
landing.

The general Australian clerical theory
appears to be that, in permitting {i.e., ordering)
the Chinese missionary-massacres, the Omnipo-
tent was only breaking eggs to make omelettes.
# • «
The executive committee of the Church
Missionary Association is going to honor in the
usual way the memories of the missionaries mas-
sacred at Ku-Cheng. In other words, it has been
decided to take up a collection.
• # #
C. P. Hodges, Chinese interpreter and
Mandarin of the Crystal Button, says that the
reparation for the Ku-Cheng massacres will be
that a few men, prompted by filial duty, will con-
sent to be beheaded on a guarantee that their
parents will be provided for during life.
• # #
While booming female missions to the
Heathen Chinee, Sydney ladies might remember
that in N.S. W. are numerous spots where their
surplus energy might well be expended. For
instance, Liverpool Asylum, where nearly a thou-
sand hopelessly decrepit men are housed—at
least 400 being in hospital. The only lady visitors
are the Sisters from the local convent. In Parra-
matta Asylums also are to be found a great
number who rarely receive a kindly visit from
anybody, male or female.
# » •
The Chinese are the most liberal sub-
scribers to the Melb. hospital. Their subscrip-
tions, in proportion to their wealth and numbers,
far exceed those of any other class. At the
elections for the medical staff, on Thursday last,
50 or CO of them voted in a body for Dr. Boyd,
the young surgeon who made the discovery of the
poisoning of Louis Juncken, which led to the
execution of Martha Needle, the Richmond lodg-
ing-house keeper
• * *
Last week, the N.S.W. board of advice to
the China Inland Mission, considered applications
from three male volunteer missionaries and one
female ditto. Two of these will probably be des-
patched—to be despatched. And, of course, one
of the twain is the female —“ an ewe lamb of tin
flock, without spot or blemish.”

It is related that a business employe of a
“great ” Australian daily lately bought a bicycle
with a view to going the rounds, and asked the
proprietor, in consideration of the saving of tram-
fares, to subsidise the machine. “Sir,” said the
manager, “the use of a bicycle would not be con-
sistent with the—er —Dignity of the Morning
Tinpot.” Like the Pope, that manager knows a
thing or two. The man who rides a bicycle
thereby invites exclusive attention to his legs, and
the legs of the average man, like those of the
average woman, really aren’t worth looking at.
* * •
The Sydney Scotchman who shared
lodgings with another man suffering from typhoid
takes the cake—for Scotchness. Scottie (a life-
assurance man), very careful of his own health,
was told by the doctor that his co-lodger must die
that night, and as a matter of duty to himself as
an official of the co. in which his fellow-lodger,
the landlord and the lady of the house were all
insured, he ordered and paid for a coffin for the
dying man, so that no time might be lost in re-
moving a possible source of infection. The
patient recovered, after all, but it was a year
before Scottie dared to mention the matter of the
coffin to his pal. Even then he funked doing it
verbally, but eventually he posted to him the
following account (verbatim):
To
Polished oak coffin, satin-lined, nickel-plated
furniture .. .. .. .. £2O 0 0
Twelve months interest at 8 per cent. .. 1 12 0
£2l 12 9
A cheque willjoblige.
* * *
The last prisoner hanged in Darling-
hurst gaol had, when taken out of the con-
demned cell for his daily exercise, to pass and
repass the prison morgue or “dead-house,” where
his own remains would shortly be laid out. This
refinement of torture was accentuated by the pro-
minent way in which the purpose of the building
is indicated. It is an ivy-covered stone structure,
over the door of which are sculptured, in high
relief, a huge grinning skull and crossbones,
flanked by an extinguisher and an hour-glass.
And the last thing seen by a N.S,W. condamne is
a noseless hangman with gorilla arms. Ugh !
Yet the fear of the gallows doesn’t prevent murder
any more than the fear of hell prevents sin.
# # #
The contents of documents found on an
arrested man do not seem to be officially regarded
as confidential, even if they have no criminal
bearing. A year ago, a man having fallen into the
hands of a Northern detective for false pretences,
he was searched in the usual manner and his
papers taken over by the police. Next day he
was brought before the Court and remanded for a
week, his bail being fixed at a certain amount,
which was forthcoming from the man at whose
residence prisoner lodged. But the police, for
some reason, objected to the release of accused,
and interviewed the bailor to dissuade him from
his intention. He, however, was firm, and an-
nounced that nothing on earth would make him
alter his mind. He believed in the man’s inno-
cence, he knew ho would not attempt to abscond,
and, as he had always been a good friend to him,
he was going to stand by him. Seeing him deter-
mined, the wily D. produced a love-letter osten-
sibly written by the bailor’s wife to the imprisoned
lodger—the lodger again !it is always the lodger
—expressing the warmest affection for him and
referring to her lawful hubby as a “ bald-headed
old scoundrel.” “ Are yer going ter bail him out
noxv ?” asked the detective. “ No,” answered the
injured one, jamming on his hat; “ I’m going to
look for my wife.”
* « «-
A bank-clerk was lately suspected of
“ going through ” the pockets of the other clerks’
coats. A detective was engaged and secreted
himself in the room where these were hanging.
The suspected man came, and, sure enough, began
operations, turning out pockets for stray coins
and tram tickets, and all the while humming to
himself, “Nearer my God to Thee !”
* * *
Thus a Colonia Cosmd man to a Bulletin
correspondent:—“ There is a most peculiar inver-
sion in the relations of the sexes in this country.
You will meet a woman driving a team of bullocks
and carrying a baby at the same time, while the
man lolls at ease in the waggon. I have seen a
woman walking along with an enormous cigar
planted behind her ear and puffing another in her
mouth. The soil is excellent. The other day an
Englishman asked a native what was the right
time to plough, and the native told him he had
been eight years on the soil without ploughing,
but he would start in about two years.”
* # #
A Bulletin correspondent writes from
Samoa : —We have exceedingly cheerful news from
Lane in Paraguay. Cosme flourishing as well as
possible time and circumstances considered.
Abundance of food and milk, and over 100 acres
ot cleared land ready for planting. By the way,
did you hear, at the time of Mrs. Lane’s start,
that Casey having refused her passage-money
from the sale of the Royal Tar, several of the
seamen, whose wages had only then been paid
up, clubbed together and brought their little all
that she might .rejoin her husband ? Amid a
general display of selfishness, that sort of thing
revives faith in humanity !

That the supply of local sawbones has
overtaken the demand, and a bit more, is appar-
ently evidenced by the fact that Sydney Univer-
sity Senate asks N.S. W. Government to beg the
British Government to let it nominate three
doctors to posts in the British army, British navy,
and Indian army respectively.

Last week’s Sydney divorce business was
agreeably varied by the granting of rules nisi on
the application of five husbands who complained
that their wives had deserted them. The ground
of desertion only has seldom been previously pre-
ferred by a male petitioner. Five deserted hus-
bands !
• * *
To translate -Judge Windeyer’s language
into plain English : The public naturally suspects
the lawyer who, when moving for a divorce, gets
into the witness-box and swears that the co-
respondent came secretly to his office arid ad-
mitted the commission of sinister acts. In any
case, it is highly improbable that a co-respondent
would visit a hostile solicitor merely to enable
that solicitor to complete a case against him ; also,
when a co-respondent does so, there is a strong
smell of “collusion” about. Further, if a co-
respondent does so, and afterwards denies that he
did so, it is a spring-balance case of oath against
oath—a lawyer’s against a layman’s.
.• • •
A female divorce petitioner appeared
before Windeyer, J., last week, in Sydney, wear-
ing a hat-band bearing, in large gold letters, the
word —“Liberty!” She gained her suit, and may
now be manoeuvring for asccond term of “slavery.”
A lot of petitioners, by the way, might brand
their hats “License.”
In N.S. Wales Divorce Court last week
divorces were granted at about the rate of 15 per
diem.
# # *
Since the recent export of young ladies
(engaged) to the Indian marriage-market, there
has been quite a rush of lovely debutantes to that
favored climate in hopes of making matches, as
the art seems to have died out in Victoria. For-
merly, India was a sure and certain market for
eligible girls ; nowadays, with the rupee at Is. Id.,
and the ever-present scare of another insurrection,
the country is not the happy hunting-ground of 30
years ago. Still, the prospect is better than that of
China.
• • *
The professional feuds of Scotch and Irish
doctors have passed into a proverb in N.S. Wales.
What is now “ popping up ” in Sydney is the dis-
tinctive jealousy between Scotch and Irish—or,
say, Scottish-Australian and Irish-Australian —
lawyers. If Scotch doctors seem to prevail in
medicine, the Irish lawyers have it mostly their
own way.
# * •
It is finally decided that Brassey is to be
landed at Deeming’s pier, St. Kilda, The Lud is
about due at the time when the aroma of the
stranded shell-fish begins to take possession of the
Victorian Ghetto, and Brassey is bound to dis-
cover that St. Kilda’s strength lies in her mussels.
There’s the difficulty : you cannot land from the
water anywhere within convenient reach of Mel-
bourne without striking a paralysing odor of some
kind. There’s a flavor of scent per scent all over
the shop. The aroma of the boom clings to the
place. By the way, Vanity Fair, possibly with
a sneer, suggests that Brassey will find Melbourne
and Melbourne society “quite to his ta.ste.”
Evidently his lordship’s smell is not considered.
# m #
Months ago The Bulletin stated that, if
the true story of the Butler (Sydney) divorce-case
were told, it would shake up local society. So
far, three of the active conspirators have been
sentenced to an aggregate imprisonment of 18
years; proceedings are being initiated against a
solicitor ; Mrs. Butler’s brother seems likely to
be released from prison on the grounds of wrong-
ful conviction ; and the body of John Copp,
Butler’s former employer, a chemist, who died
rather suddenly some four years ago, m'ay possibly
be exhumed.
C"pp, a chemist, of Darlington, Sydney, the
circumstances of whose death are said to have been
su-pioious, was a born naturalist, and devoted all
his spare time to the stocking of a museum at the
back of his shop. Botanists and naturalists have
come from all quarters to view his collection,
which contained some of the rarest and costliest
specimens of the animal and vegetable world. But
it is altogether unlikely that, if deceased was
poisoned as alleged, the fact could now be fully
established. The theory of those who suspect
Copp to have been murdered fits in only with the
administration of a vegetable, evanescent poison.
The gentle Kanaka who comes to Queens-
land for the benefit of his immortal soul, set upon
a white man at Mackay, a month ago, and
literally carved him into mincemeat. Fifty
hideous gashes did the doctor count on the unfor-
tunate white man’s carcase ; and Mackay is peti-
tioning to get the Polynesians locked up every
night on the plantations. The blackbird-traffic
isn’t slavery.

# • #
“How is the curse of Deeming getting
on ?’’ This instance of it is as yet unrecorded.
Two days before the hanging, the murderer’s
attorney fell in witii a party of footballers at
Mordialloc. He had a drink with them, and saw
them off in the boat by which they were going to
Mornington to play a match. They pressed him
to accompany them, but Mr. Lyle declined. That
boating party encountered the full weight of the
curse. The boat was upset, and every man in it
drowned.
# # •
Very tardily Vic. authorities are resur-
recting the smellful Synnot case by instituting an
enquiry into the evidence given at the trial by-
three constables. It has just occurred to some-
body that whether the badly-broken-up testimony
against Mrs. Synnot was largely perjury, or an
extraordinary instance of mistaken identity, the
policemen showed up badly. If perjury, they
were rogues; if mistaken identity, they were
fools; and, therefore, they are dangerous as police-
men, anyhow.

In Melbourne they have lowered the tax
On corsets to a small per cent.,
But if the duty were more lax
Protectionists would be content.
They find where womankind abound
Good proof that corsets would not fail
To be severely taxed all round
Whatever tariff might prevail.

Re the unreliability of girl witnesses : In
the case of a man charged before Vic. Justice Hood
with criminally assaulting his daughter, aged 14,
a younger daughter, aged 11, stated that she saw
her father enter the room and commit the assault,
but admitted, under cross-examination, that her
statement was untrue, and she only made it be-
cause Auntie told me to.” ‘• Auntie told me sle
would give me a silk dress and a hat, and shoes if
I told lies of father.” This sort of thing has
become-so common in the courts as to make i
questionable whether the unsupported testimony
of girls under the age of consent should evei be
admitted, *
m *
Joseph Cook, the parson from Boston
(U.S.A.), is not only a sky-pilot but aims also at
being an earth-pilot. Says he : “ The chief article
in the commercial life of the world is religion.
Wrong The chief article in the commercial life
of the world is Cant, labelled “Religion. . this
spuriously-labelled Cant is a veritable article of
commerce : it is bought and sold and haggled
over, It is branded like the soaps of commerce,
and every brander declares that his own brand is
not only the best, but the only genuine reli-
gion ” in the market. But, make no mistake,
real religion never gets into the market, never
comes nearer “ commercial life than ftinus or
Aldebaran. .

Dr. Manning, of the N.S.W. lunacy
bureau, regrets the yearly increase of insanity in
the province. The wonder, however, is that three-
fourths Svdney isn’t lunatic. Nearly every house
in every “suburb has several dogs that yelp all
round the clock, and one, two, or twenty hell-
imps who make night and day hideous with the
accursed rumty-tum. And while the rumty-tum
plague thrives, the peaceful and industrious
citizen will change to a raving madman, whose
bloodshot eye shall roll in vacancy and whose
frothy tongue shall blaspheme the universe.
New form of death-certificate. ; “ Cause of
death (primary), Rurnty-tum ; (secondary)]Land-
boom.”

Dear Bulletin, —Here’s one hona-f.de miss
of a doomed ship. I once saw, at Derry, a batch
of about 50 excited Mayo peasants, the men
ferocious and the women virulent because, after
travelling 00 miles, they had barely missed their
passage to Canada by a M.O.S.S. boat —which
vessel, with 400 souls on board, was never heard
of again.—M.

Rev. Dr. Gordon, of the Presbytery of
Melbourne South, is anxious to see the Vic.
Licensing Act amended with a view to the aboli-
tion of barmaids and of window-blinds in public-
houses. This shepherd thinks that men would
not drink at bars if they were left in full view of
people passing. A law making it compulsory for
publicans to sprinkle their bars with asafmtida
several times a day, or to employ only Chinese
lepers to serve out drinks, would be more likely to
have the effect desired by the rev. reformer, and
he is welcome to make what use he pleases of IHR
Bulletin’s suggestion. At the same time, the
Licensed Victuallers should hasten to agitate for
the immediate abolition of pretty female teachers
in Sunday schools, lovely choristers in churches,
and also comely chambermaids in coffee-palaces—
which are ten times more dangerous to female
virtue than decent hotels —and for an Act pro-
hibiting the holding of sacerdotal picnics in
scrubby places, and restricting these at fresco
festivities to the centre of wide and barren plains
where the bilious superintendent and the coy
soprano may always be distinctly visible to the
naked eye at a distance of several miles.

The total wealth of the Rothschild family
is estimated at 41400,000,000,
Let politics simply go to pot,
And let your mind be aisy,
And look at the slice of luck we've got.
When Dorothy Brand’s a daisy !
And every dude a chance will stand
And use the arts at his command
To gain the hand of Dorothy Brand,
For Dorothy Brand’s a daisy !
Now, Governors’ girls are mostly plain,
A lolloping lot, and lazy,
But here, let’s tell you over again,
Is Dorothy Brand, the daisy !
The Hampden girls have lots of sand,
There isn’t an ugly one in the band,
But Dorothy Brand is simply grand ;
Oh, Dorothy Brand’s a daisy !

No notice will he taken of communications insuffi-
ciently stamped. Name and address must
appear on MS. No liability is undertaken re-
contributions voluntarily submitted , and in no
case will MS. be returned unless stamps (oj
any province) accompany.

The Argus upholds London Punch as
“ a humorous and gentlemanly” paper. And
that is what is also the matter with the Argus.
Smoke Eagle Cigarettes.*
The interest in Cellular Clothing is be
coming widespread. The extent of application o f
Cellular fabric is so large that it may be said to
cover every variety of clothing for all seasons and
for all climates. Since the doctors recommend it,
and since it gives the maximum warmth with the
minimum weight, and is therefore peculiarly suit-
able for Australian climates, it is not surprising
to find it quickly superseding the old materials
used for underclothing. The sole agents for the
colony of Victoria are Messrs. Hicks, Atkinson
and Sons, of Collins-street. Economy is also
among the virtues of Cellular Clothing.*
The only known cure for influenza is Cole-
mane and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
Things political are likely to be a bit more
lively shortly in Victoria. The stagnation has
been stirred up by Mr. H. H. Champion’s new
weekly paper, The Champion, which looks as if
it was going to achieve the impossible and estab-
lish itself as an independent democratic journal in
that province. There are some pens on the staff
that know how to write. A sample copy of the
paper will be posted to anyone who sends a post-
card to 7 Queen’s Walk, Melbourne; or a ss. postal
order to the same address will bring it to you
every Thursday for six months.*

“ Crom-a-boo,” that funniest of Irish comic
football songs, is selling faster than ever.*
