August 20, 1895.
ftiv Dbah Moorabinda—
The Military Papprchase across tlie harmless
Botany flats last Thursday was doubtless a
“merry run ” from the point of view of a girl who
/ides with her heel up ; but some of the “ timber-
lakers” present found the innocuous canter
lit rough the scrub to Long Bay a rather unexciting
Say’s work under Y.R. patronage. Acting-
Governoress D.trley drove to the tryst, accom-
panied by Miss Kitty Darley, in a black-and-blue
itriped gown and a blue neck ruffle ; Miss Sylvia
Barley drove too, in the sequel, for her cream
pony turned Turk in the part, and she followed
she run in a dog-cart. Tea awaited the chasers
at the finish, and, after tea, the whole company
were photographed on foot—front view, fortu-
nately for an equestrienne in tan jack boots and
one of the “safety” riding-skirts, which are like
an apron, split up the back till further orders,
and so do not lend themselves to promenading in
mixed company. The rear-view of this damsel
(who cut a very neat and pretty figure in the
saddle) was the most astonishing thing in splits I
have seen since Florence Young wore that rose-
trimmed black gown in Madame Angot. After
all, society girls have their duly-authorised little
ways of getting even with the burlesque stage.
But all the sartorial interest wasn’t on the
feminine side. Major-General Hutton turned out
in a hat with a “ poultice ’’ on it, and a check-
suit that ought certainly to be liequeathed to the
nearest low-comedian when the M.G, takes his
departure. Though, of course, checks are all the
go, horsily speaking, even if they don’t suit every-
body. A very popular youth most unwisely wore
a Tattersall-check vest which increased his waist
to 1 alstafflan proportions, though his extra-roomy
riding-pants showed great perspicuity on his
tailor s part, I must say. Air. Cecil Hope’s most
Striking feature was his yellow breeches, the color
of his dreams when he dreams of Coolgardie, but
the newest thing in masculine leg-wear was a pair
of black tights and black, close-fitting gaiters.
They looked as if the Attorney-General let out
his official garments on hire during the close
season. But those legs were an anachronism
on a horse—Providence manifestly intended ’em
for a bicycle. A number of men drove.
The opening of Parliament, on 14th inst., by
the Acting-Governor, who made his debut as a
speechifier from the Throne on that occasion, was
not so effective as usual owing to the finery being
at half-pressure, because of the absence of Simon
Pure vice-royalty. Acting-Gov. Darley, in a tight
blue uniform with brass buttons and a cocked hat
devoid of plumage, suggested a glorified police-
man, but he laid down the political programme
with great art, in an almost indistinct voice
which was altogether en suite with the subject-
matter. Parliament set a-movin’ by the Acting-
Gov. is like a rooster without his tail feathers.
Splendor is infectious; but when tiie Throne
merely suggests the “foorce,” legislators who
ought to sport warpaint taper off to morning
dress without disturbing the eternal fitness. So
there wasn’t much of a peepshow for the crowd of
women assembled (with the Acting-Governoress
in a pink bonnet in the van), except, pirhaps, the
ballet legs of one or two high officials in black
stockings, which lent an air of burlesque to the
proceedings. Another entertaining feature was
the good-looking A. V.R. secretary in full evening-
dress, standing motionless against the crimson
background behind the Throne, His petrified
pose with the Speech in one hand suggested a per-
son undergoing the worst form of photography.
However, the opening deserves some fame as
being the only public gathering of any importance
at which the Gee-Gees were not represented.
The Enriquez benefit-concert, tendered by
friends and admirers, came off at Sydney Cen-
tenary Hull, on 14th inst., with the indefatigable
acting-Governoress in a pink cloak, a daughter or
so, and an obviously bored aide-de-camp, under
the close surveillance of an audience which didn't
nearly fill the hall. Ihe ben. was not a success
financially, although the programme was very well
compiled—the contralto being down for four num-
bers without prejudice to possible encores, while
Alias Colborne-Baber (handsomely gowned in
yellow brocade), Madame Kellermann (in green
velvet and striped silk), Mr. Frank Adams, Dr.
Maguire, a tuneful bevy from theLiedertafel, Mr.
Donovan, Edgar ’cello Strauss, and Alons. Pou«-
sard were all actively concerned in the programme.
The platform was neatly decorated with pot*
plants draped in danger-signals, and bouquet*
went up by the barrow-load. The only weakness
lay in the takings.
One of Madame Enriquez’ items (she was
gowned in white brocade, by the way, with sacri-
ficial garlands of scarlet roses and a most
display of diamonds) was a song by Dr. AlcCarthjr
entitled “Still Apart.” I couldn't hear the
words, but the whole thing was too long. With a
few bars added it would have made a very re-
spectable cantata, but, regarded as a song, it
decidedly howls for condensation. The refrain,
too, is distinctly joyous, suggesting that the singer
doesn’t particularly object to the separation. You
picture the title-page as bearing the legend “Still
Apart, thankfully dedicated to my mother-in-
law.” Aliss Col bourne Baber was encored for her
solo—an “ Ave Maria,” with organ and ’cello
obligati—to which she responded; but Dr. Alaguire,
of the suave and polished baritone, refused a triple
encore for his refined singing of a Verdi recit.
and aria. J. E. Sykes and Harrison White were
accompanists-in-general. Apropos of concerts, a
very big one, under vice-vice-regal patronage, is
now on the carpet, to be held in the Town Hall
in .aid of the Boys’ Brigade. Taking it “by and
large,” Sydney professional musicians have no
reason to complain of a lack of opportunity to
exhibit the result of their hard work—gratis.
Sydney Theosophists would not seem to be a
very ’thusiastic handful, considering how sparsely
the Countess of Wachtmeister’s at home on Satur-
day* at the Theo. Society’s rooms, was attended.
The function suggested a number of patients
chumming in to while away the time in a doctor’s
waiting-room—an idea which the frankly dreary
surroundings, rigid pictures, hard-and-fast tables
without covers, and no airy ornaments—distinctly
encouraged. However, if Theosophy is not a beau-
tifully-furnished religion, it has at least the
wondrous power of making its male disciples very
handy about the house, so to speak. At the
reception on Saturday tea was altogether mani-
pulated by the “ brothers’’—the women sitting
round and enjoying the spectacle of men, who
never do more than trot round with the served
beverage, peering into the tea-pot and deciding
the vexed question as to whether the mixture
would stand dilution or not —quite d la hostess.
A Theosophist would apparently make an ideal
husband for the woman who likes a cup of tea
before rising.
But, though the company was decidedly flabby
and inert—having to be entertained in sections by
the Countess, or the good-looking he-secretary, in
the orthodox Theospphical murmur—the thing
was worth while, because Countess Wachtmeister
is a most charming woman. I do not mean it
physically ; nobody could be Venus with mouse-
colored hair, drawn tightly back from the fore-
head, and fastened in a bathroom sort of bob at
the back—a plainly-made dress of black-figured
stuff—no white collar, and a tiny Theosophical
brooch. Rings, of course, though not so numerous
or so fantastic as Annie B-sant’s— but Countess
W. owes little of her charm to her get-up. Her
voice and manner are her strong points. She
suggests Annie Besant under the influence of
morphia, for her style is dreamier than that of the
lady with the chameleonic hair, though her facts
and figures are just as wide-awake. Her style
suggests bullets wrapped in wadding.
I had a little gossip with Mr. John Alarqnardt
—celebrated as a violinist—and Aline. Breitsohuck-
Alarqnardt—equally renowned ns a performer on
that celestial instrument, the harp—at their hotel
the other morning. Madame is a good-looking
brunette (somewhat reminiscent of Italia del
Torre) who wears her hair in a Venus knot, and
has her taper fingers covered with diamond rings.
The harp has not spoilt the shape of her finger-
tips, because inadame possesses the gift of a
dry hand, without which harping is a vain
thing. Her favorite composer is, of course.
Parish Alvars, the “ Beethoven of the harp”
—the sweetest instrument in the world in the hands
of an artist, and one of which ’ Australia has but
a single considerable exponent up-to-date—John
Barker, of Alelbourne. Air. Alarqnardt looks
more like an operatic tenor than a violinist, but
his musical style is evidently not so fierce as his
moustache, his average being one burst fiddle-
string to three concerts, Churubill Orton manages
the tour, which starts at the Town Hall on Satur-
day.
From W, 11. Paling and Co. I have received a
copy of the Zarita waltz, by Sara E. Bear. The
Zanta is a tinkly waltz, suggestive of tambourines
and cymbals—all ouite in accordance with the
Eastern-looking title-page, (nit there really is no
special reason (considering the state of the waltz-
market) why the composer should have hastened
to introduce Zarita to a weary public. Another
new thing is a “Dance of Dryads,” arranged for
piano from the S. A.O S. fantasia, “Night in the
Forest”—by C. S. Childe, It is a valiant effort
in a smallpox cover, proving that the able-bodied
person with strong fists may make almost ss much
noise as a whole orchestra, if you show him the
way, and, in competent hands, the Dance (even
Dryads dance in 3-4 time, alas !) would he a very
taking thing in fantasias. It is not difficult—
airily speaking. Occasionally the ground-plan
suggests aChine.se battle, hut fantasias are always
built that way. I have also received a copy of
“Still Apart,” the song by Dr. McCarthy, men-
tioned above as sung by Madame Enriquez. The
maudlin words are by Arthur Rigby ; and yon
would never dream, judging from “ Still Apart,”
what an exceptionally gifted musician Dr.
AlcCarthy occasionally is, /
I think'the most astonishing thing about the
recent dreadful missionary-massacres in China is
that the Chinese included the missionesses in the
butcher’s bill, for there always seems to be so
much cordial feeling between Chiiiamanee and
E ilopean miasee—outside China,'at least. IndeFd,
no main of the world would ever allow his woman-
kind to have anything whatsoever to do with
Chinamen. The experience of society in San
Francisco, where Chinamen are numerous and
many well-educated women have* engaged in their
“conversion,” is that, in an enormous percentage
of eases, the pious pow- wow ends with the well-
meaning woman going wrong with the yellow-skin
—and even in Sydney the fatal; fascinations of
Flowery Land beaux are not absolutely unfelt.
In a Southern suburb a good-looking, well-
educated girl—the daughter of eminently respect-
able people—is engaged to be married to a howling
heathen from the land of tea, and appears to quite
glory in the fact, despite the remonstrances of her
circle—who are probably jealous of her. The case
instanced in “The Gaiety Girl,” of the poor
woman “who fell in love with a stockbroker,” is
a mere nothing to this, though, when yon come to
look at it, the thing isn't so very wonderful.
Women adore politeness and suavity, and the
graceful insight into feminine nature which
teaches John to mark his cabbage above market-
rates soi as to give bis fair customers the pleasure
of beating him down to his own price, shows a
keen appreciation of onr sex which ramifies far
beyond mere vegetable-barter. Let every woman
ask herself in her heart of hearts whether she
likes her vegetable John or not. I’ll bet a cabbage
what her answer will be.
But it isn’t altogether wonderful that women
should be so hypnotised if they once get on visit-
ing-tenns—religious or not —with Nanki - Poo.
Really, when you come to contrast their appear-
ance, at any rate, there isn’t so very much differ-
ence between a Chinaman and an average British
naval officer—now, is there ? Both are clean-
shaven and tan-skinned—both exercise an in-
describable and somewhat unaccountable attrac-
tion for the fair sex. No man of the world would
allow his women-folk to start a society for the
conversion of naval officers, involving frequent
visits on board for prayer and expostulation, any
more than he would for the conversion of Ah Sin.
That most benevolent and popular clergyman,
the Rev. A. H. Bartlett (Phil May’s parson-model
for the bewildered cleric in “the Parson and the
Painter,” who went round London and Paris
together in a shower of brilliant sketches), lias
lately been presented by his adoring Burwood
(Sydney) congregation with several farewell gifts,
ranging from a reading-lamp to a silver match-
box. A handsome diamond bracelet was also pro-
vided for madame. It was a wicked thing for
Phil May to use the Burwood clergyman as a
model in such a connection, for M. Bartlett is, in
the very best sense of the word, a model parson.
Brisbane is bis new sphere.
My Melbourne correspondent :—A lately-im-
ported official matron, who ran the gauntlet of
popular curiosity at her initial receptions, and was
visited by pretty nearly everyone, atoned for it
afterwards by the “fun” she derived from the
process of “returning the calls.” No visitor was
too obscure to escape detection ; she unearthed
them all, enthroned in her State carriage and
attended by lackeys, who sniffed scornfully at the
poky villas and doublefrontage cottages “dis-
covered ” cn route. The doors were opened in
many cases by the hostess herself, hot from the
ironing-table or redolent of cookery, and—but
■details would be too harrowing.
“A late BULLETIN ‘par.,’ describing Miss
Maggie Sterling, Melbourne’s popular mezzo-
soprano, as a ‘ handsome copy of Lady Hope-
tonn ’is a little misleading. The resemblance is
striking, certainly, though the singer, lacking her
ex-Exoellency’s warm, brown complexion, suggests,
irather, a p illid, pensive, eld r sister —which is not
to be wondered at, considering that her beautiful
Yoice is the sole support of her invalid husband
and three young children.
“ A recent lecture, abounding in reminiscences,
rank and racy, still sticks in the throats of its
hearers. The chairman, a legal authority, young,
ingenuous and pretty, apparently felt his position
keenly at the time; and ever since he has been
bombarded with letters from infuriated purists,
Avho henceforth will doubtless regard him with
suspicion as a menace to the morality of the com-
munity. •
“Other sufferers from the spiciness of the lecture
are those unfortunates who, unsuspectingly, ob-
tamed invitations for serious acquaintances. One
such victim, publicly reproved for Ids ‘ low-down
conduct,’ was finally laid out by the remark,
‘ And you a member of Mr. Chancel’s church 1 ’
“The prettiest girl at the recent Cliveden ‘at
home ’ was, I thought, the daughter of the house
—tall, fair and graceful, in a white silk crepon ;
tieing for second place came two sisters, the
younger of whom was one of the debutantes at
the last Govt. House ball. These young ladies,
relatives of a well-known dentist, wore silver-
sprigged white brocade, and silvery aigrettes and
.shoulder-straps. Melbourne's leading military
dame was conspicuous in a Satanic toilette of
scarlet, with upstanding black wings in her hair.
“It would be hard to foretell the result of a
local competition for a vacant pro-consulship,
but could the position of vice-regal ‘leading
lady’ be contested ‘on its own,’ Cliveden’s
sprightly mistress would probably head the
poll. Unlike many of their imported lady-
ships, whose society demeanour, as often as not,
suggests an urchin commanded to ‘ look plea-
sant ’ {immediately after a spanking, smiles and
small talk come naturally to her. At the concert,
the other evening, there were few who did not
envy her convenient knack of spontaneous
laughter, smoothing over, as it did, the otherwise
chilling reception of a series of society sketches,
which would have been hopelessly behind the times
10 years ago. The impersonation of the little
girl, reciting the ‘ Psalm of Life’ in a shy, sing-
song gabble, was especially out of date, present-
day youngsters, stagey in picture-frocks and
fluffed hair, would scorn sucb a railk-and-watery
performance, preferring to rave and tear through
a dramatic selection like Owen Meredith’s ‘Por-
trait,’ airing their ‘society English’ in such
lines as
Oit, Ood ! 'twas the Raphael-faced young priest
Who shraved her when she da-ed 1
“Early Victorian fashions, 'tis said, conduce tc
modesty, because, minus the support of a shoulder-
strap, the bodice must be cut within hail of the
collar-bone to keep up at all. At a recent fashion-
able assemblage, however, a mature, upper-crusiy
spinster, equally devoted to society junketings
and good works, though up-to-date as far as
sleeves were concerned, was nevertheless quite her
old self, her ‘lB3o’ bodice strongly re-
sembling a small riding-corset in bright blue
satin.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
SPOUTING NOTIONS.
Murray Smith boasted in the Vic, tote-debate
that he had put this poser to the racina men who had
canvassed him on behalf of the machine: “ What is th••
difference between the loialisalor and Monte Carlo?”
Murray Smith might a>k what is the difference between
himself and the greatest drunkard on earth ?-they both
drink. What is the difference between a suicide-club and
an art-union ? it is a toss-up in each case. What is the
difference between a marine-insurance policy and a
Chinese lottery-ticket? What is the difference between
Freetrade and robbery-nnder-aiins? What is tire differ-
ence between a liar and a novelist and a lawyer and a
politician?—it is all a matter of degree. What is the
difference between a gambling-machine which pla.i g fair
and which only operates at occasional big meetings,
and a saturnalia of gambling which lasts day and night
all through the year! ! 1

Ikey Mo was a fair goer on the eve of the tote
debate in V'ic. Assembly, llis precious Sportsman, and
its blond relation the ’Kralu, pelted the coining Bill with
dead-pat arguments in all stages of putrefaction. The
voice of Ikey’s own particular special pleader, and the
snuffle of many a nobbled parson, could he heard through-
out the I mil. Ikey himself worked like a Jew possessed; ’
plying Councillors with further instructions to stand firm
when the Bill reached the Unpp reuse, rushing round to
hotel-keepers and exhorting ihem to preach the awful
“ immorality ” of the hlankv appa at ns to every customer,
and borrowing, perhaps, the key of the Synagogue that
he might go within and pray hard for the tote’s discom-
fiture. Melbourne, Bendigo, and Ballarat had never
before known so much Ikey in the air as they experienced
last week. The immortal soul of !. Moses seemed to he
hovering overhead like an angry crow that sees its carrion
in danger, and one could almost hear the evil spirit flap-
ping a black wing against a black hag and sereeihing,
“In the name of ehurch morality, let me live on! ” at. the
moment when 35 members of the Assembly fluttered 49
farcical petitions, signed by 2000 publicans and parsons
in all, against the Legislative sanctioning of Ikey’s exter-
mination.

‘The Australian Mining Standard.’
To the Subscribers and Advertisers. —
I have pleasure in notifying you that I have
purchased the Mining STANDARD, and intend
issuing it at an early date as an illustrated jour-
nal. Until I have secured suitable premises, and
made the necessary arrangements for its publica-
tion under the new conditions, it will appear as
heretofore. ... .
The Mining Standard has up to the present
received the practical support of those interested
in the great industry it represents, and I trust
that when I put before yon the comprehensive
scheme now formulating, one business relations
will continue and increase upon a mutually satis-
factory basis. CRITCHLEY PARKER.
Sydney, August 13th, 1895.

After the 25 to 5 defeat of the Victorian Rugby
team by the Northern Union, the return match against
va vv at Sydney Cricket Ground last Saturday, had a
foregone conclusion. The Vies, began with a strong wind
behind them, but could take not the least advantage of it,
for none of their backs can kick at all. Their forwards
played fairly until they got near the N.S.W. hne, and then
they went to pieces. Moreover, they passed badly, and
seemed to have a mania for dribbling. With half-time
NSW. had scored 6 to 0. In the interval the [Sydney
Bi'cvole Club’s Three-Mile handicap was easily won by the
scratch man, Macdonald; five or six riders fell towards
the fl"Uh, one breaking his collar-bone. The Rugby
“match ” in the second half, degenerated into a scramble,
none of’the N.S.W. men taking it seriously except
MacMahon, who heavily grassed a couple of the Victorians.
N.S.W. won by 19 to 5.
* * * *
The return football-match between Queensland
and N.S.W. brought a surprising win for N.S.V\ by 11 to
8 : the previous Saturday Q. had won by 26 to 16. the
n’s W forwards played with combined aggression, and
both their tries in the first half were obtained by passing
rushes. With the first 15 minutes of the second half the
crowd’s excitement reached its height, for the Queens-
landers had made scores equal. But from that out
N S W., continually pressing upon its opponents, had the
game fairly in hand, and won more comfortably than is
inferable from the scores.
* • * *
About the best Vic. football of the season was
played in the Geelong v. Essendon match on Saturday,
both teams keeping at concert pilch throughout. Essen-
dons were superior to their opponents, except during one
quarter when Geelong put on 5 goals (3 kicked by Deceit)
in 12 minutes. Esaendonsovershadowed and played their
rivals to a standstill in the last quarter, and with ten
minutes mere would certainly have won.
* * * *
Geelong, provided they don’t go to pieces in the
meantime, seem “dead birds” for the Vic. premiership.
Although Fitzroy beat Melh, on Saturday, either team is
outclassed by Essendon or Geelong. Fitzroy s four uoals
to two against Melb. is a fair play criterion of that par-
ticular match, hut not of the teams. As a matter of fact,
Melh. men were in such a mediocre level that great
difficulty was experienced on finding the winner of Kelly
Bros.' trophy for the best Melb. player.
The racy-looking M.L. Maxim colt, Mann-
licber, promises to start first favorite for the A.J.U
Derby. After arrival, he was placed on the same betting-
mark as Bob Ray-at fours to one. The only thing against
Mannlioher is that he won’t be really three years old
until the end of Nov., being a late foal.

Secretary Tom Furse is again “the man
at tl e helm ” for this year’s demonstration ot
Labor at Sydney. In another column he sets
forth the manifold advantages of buying a tick* t,
which not only admits to the gala to be held at,
Rosehill on Oct. 7, but gives the holder a chance
of landing one of the many Art Union prizes, the
first and richest of which is .£5OO, or equiva-
lent. The trading public are responding well to
the call for subscriptions in cash and trophies, and
the prizedist in the sports departments is the
heaviest yet set out. A novel and soul-satisfying
attraction will be the grand-band contest, for
which the first prize is .£5O, the second .£3O, and
the third .£lO. Ten bands must enter or there
will be no race, and with ten bands doing battle
there ought to be noise and fury enough to scare
the obstructing devils out of seven counties. Mr.
Furse is a steam-engine of unlimited horsepower,
and he means to make this year s turn-out a
record. *

The chemists to the Sanitary Commission-
ers of India have been investigating alleged snake-
poison antidotes. They report that strychnine
has no power whatever when even the minimum
fata! dose is given ; paralysis of the lungs and
other usual symptoms always following. Solution
of periodate crystals, the South American fancy
article, is also alleged to be absolutely useless :
ditto chloride of gold; ditto permanganate of
potash. But, so far as strychnine is concerned,
Ur. Mueller’s experiments had just the opposite
result ; and there are now many recorded Austra-
lian cases of snake-bite cured by strychnine-injec-
tion, while in few or none has the treatment failed
when adopted at once.
A firm of bacon-curers from London is
starting a branch establishment at Lilydale (Vic.),
and has begun to introduce the “mild cure” of
V ictorian bacon to notice. An export-trade is
the principal end in view, but the mild cure seems
likely to suit the local consumer. Bacon which
presents “a juicy appearance when freshly cut,
and will be found much less salty than most Aus-
tralian bacon,”is a very desirable article for these
parts, where the old, strong, tough kind of curing
lias been carried to an extreme. Mr. Veoht, the
manager of the “London Mild Bacon Curing Co.”
thinks that his new system—or, rather, the old-
established system which he is introducing to
Australia—will be generally adopted by the trade
here as soon as its merits become known.

Melbourne suburbs have been infested
with ghosts recently, couples out taking the night
air being particularly troubled. A touching tale
comes from Albert Park. Two young people were
sitting up in the moonlight affording each other
encouragement and support, when a dreadful shape
ioomed up out of the shadow of an adjacent tree
and was about to grin horribly when the young
man went over the seat backwards, picked him-
self up, and fled through the night. He collected
all his relations and called in the assistance of the
police, and the army went back to the rescue of
the girl. The party approached the scene of
horror very gradually and found the young woman
nestling her curly head up against the bosom of
the spook, collected in the spectre’s arms, and
quite composed. “It” was the other fellow, and
he retains possession.
A Bulletin man who happens to have no
objections to meeting his tailor just now, re-
cently received the confidence of a Melb. snip
seeking sympathy. “See that dress-suit?” asked
the vulgar fraction mournfully. “ Any man with
half an eye can tell it’s been worn. I made it to
the order of a man here who was once a luminary
in Oollins-street. It was delivered the night
before the last reception at Government House,
and returned the evening after, with Mr. Blank’s
compliments, and Mr. Blank would see me hanged
before he’d wear a suit that fitted him like a
sugar-bag on a fence-rail. No further explana-
tions beyond a pretty unconscionable hint that
Blank wasn’t worth going for.” But the man of
threads and patches is certain that suit was at
Government House, and has still hopes of it going
off with judicious pressing.

Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir,—ln the year of 1871 I was eight days in the water sheep-
washing, and contracted rheumatism and stiffness in the joints, and
have tried all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney I had a
great difficulty in walking, and went to the Turkish baths, which did
no good. Passing your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E. W.
O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as I had, which led me to
try your Remedies. On Thursday before Good Friday I took the first
of your Pills; since then I have used 4 boxes Special Powerful Alfaline
Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with

years ago. I can with great recommend your Remedies to
anyone suffering from the same complaint. May you long be spared
under God to be a blessing to suffering humanity.

‘ THE LAST OF SIX.
The frequency and unction with which Mr.
Greer’s N.S.W. Labor Bureau delivers itself of
the report, “ The men sent into the country show a
high sense of their appreciation of the Bureau’s
services by remitting so promptly the cost of their
fare to their place of destination,” makes it neces-
sary to ask this question : “Does the Bureau send
a note marked ‘ private ’ to the employers of the
men so despatched, requesting that the money
spent by the Bureau shall bestopped from the first
or the early payments of the men, and forwarded
to Sydney?” Are such notes—marked private—
to be found up Narrabri way, and have there been
cases in which the whole of a man’s pay has been
stopped until the Bureau account was satisfied ?
A London paper, on Cowen’s new opera,
says : “The subject of King Harold, the last of
the Saxons, is essentially national, though neces-
sarily Englishmen must regard the subjugation
of our country by William of Normandy with
mixed feelings.” Even if “our country” means
Hingland, quite apart from the rest of Great
Britain, the remark is absurd enough. Norman
blood has circulated everywhere, and the people
who have avoided getting any of it into their
families, are probably Danish or Norwegian rather
than Saxon by descent. And, whatever their real
pedigree may be, they are always prepared to hug
the notion that at least one of their ancestors
came over with the Conqueror. Their feelings, if
they had any in the matter, would prompt them
to witness the collapse of Harold with a haughty
smile.
The result of the Opium Commission
(British Parliament) deliberations is startling and
of much interest to Australians. As regards the
advisability of prohibition the verdict was unani-
mously “No !’’ The Commission also found that
the people of India were totally unwilling to have
the drug prohibited and totally unwilling to bear
the cost of prohibition The most interesting
part of the report was that bearing on the effect
of the consumption of opium on the moral and
physical well-being of the people. A vast majority
of the witnesses declared the use of the drug in
moderation innocuous, and only its excess—bad.
It was the common domestic medicine of the
people. Opium (it was asserted) is the common
cure for malarial fever, dysentery and endemic
diarrhoea, and an eminent medical authority, Sir
William Roberts, maintains its efficacy. Accor-
ding to Roberts the anarcotine present in opium
is superior to quinine as an antiseptic. Witnesses
appeared who had eaten opium for 15 and 25 years
and had found it had done them good. The
hardiest races in India patronise it. (By the
way, opium-eater De Quincey lived to the
age of 74). Medical authorities testified to the
Commission that the use of alcohol was far more
injurious than the use of opium, and, generally
speaking, advised John Bull to reform his own
drunken habits before meddling with the compara-
tively harmless customs of Asiatics.
Dear Bulletin,— N.S.W. Local Option
League is anguished because N.S.W. has spent
<£45,000,000 in liquor during the last 10 years.
Well, what of it? There’s over a million people
in N.S.W. I’ll make the L.O. League a present
of. the hundreds of thousands of surplus, and am
willing, as far as I am concerned, to admit my
responsibility as one individual in a bare million.
And what is that for 10 years ? Why, a paltry
! Not enough to have got a teetotal mis-
sionary an outfit to China ! And in 10 years,
mark you : only £4 10s, a year ! Great ginger-
pop ! what does the Local Option League want?
There were two leap years in the decade ’B5-94
(inclusive). Therefore, there were 3652 days—
mostly of megrims and misery, only for the “oil ”
of gladness. Now, if I spent only 3d. per day
(Sundaysand holidays included) in that which both
cheereth and inebriateth, look you, I would have
spent 10,956 d., or £45 13s. Does the league
begrudge me one thripenny fusel oil a day ?
Perhaps they want me to make a margin for
infants? Well, let us suppose three-quarters of
the population of N.S.W. are too young, too
moral, too weak, or too otherwise unfit to swill
bug-juice. That leaves 45 millions sterling worth
of grog to be drunk and paid for by a quarter of a
million of people during 10 years. What is that?
=£45,000,000 between 250,000 people is only <£lBo
per head. And that is spread over 10 years. ,£lB
a year ! Not quite Is. a day ! Not four nips a
day ! Why, we are a sober nation, and I feel
ashamed of myself! If I had, however, saved
this shilling a day and put £lBO into the Christian
Providential Lord willprovide Building and Invest-
ment Society, would its pious reconstructing
directors now give me a shilling a day to chant
“ Amen ” to their “ Alleluias ? ” Mind you, the
£45,000,000 are their own figures, these local
option leaguers. So you see that the Curse of
Derrink is a bogey, and is to the Local Option
League what Mrs. Mellon’s spooks were to Judge
Windeyer.—Yours, The Dipsomaniac.

The Aborigines and Their Dead.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Recently Mr. H. J. M‘Cooey queried in
The Bulletin “How used N.S.W. aborigines to
bury their dead ? ” Here are a few notes
drawn from many sources. Eleven modes of
disposing of the dead were in use among the
Australian aborigines, to wit, (a) cremation, (6)
burial in the bed of a running stream, (c), suspen-
sion across the limb of a tree, (d) exposure on a
staging among branches of a tree, (e) enclosure in
a cave, (/) exposure on an artificial staging raised
on stakes, (g) burial in a disused mirrn-yong or
ash-heap, ( h) burial in the earth, ( i ) burial with
others in a stone-lined trench, (j) cannibal con-
sumption by relatives, ( k ) exposure on surface of
ground. By almost every tribe employing burial
the body was tied up into a sort of ball, with the
knees tucked up to the chin, and buried in a
squatting posture with the face to the east ; to
avoid useless repetition, therefore, only variations
from this practice will be noticed. Names of
authorities are in parentheses.
* * •
Near Perth, W.A., a grave was dug just
large enough for the body, the white sand placed
at the east and west ends, and the discolored sand
at the sides ; the corpse was wrapped in a rug, and
the grave filled up with small boughs; large
branches were laid across, and the dirty sand piled
into a mound on top (Grey). On the Vasse River,
the corpse was smeared with blood from the
mourners’ thighs and buried as near Perth, but a
hut was afterwards built over the grave (Bussell).
Near York, W. A., the Whajook tribe cut off the
hair and burned off the nails of the dead, and after
burial raised a mound over the grave, into which
green boughs and deceased’s weapons were stuck
(Goldsworthy). In the same district the Ballin-
dong natives fill up the grave with alternate layers
of bark and earth (Hackett). Ninety miles north
of Albany, among the Kojonnp and Bticup, the
thumb-nails were burned off, the right forefinger
and thumb tied together, and thegrave was filled up
with bark, &c., the dug-up earth never being re-
turned (Graham). Near King George’s Sound,
the grave was short, narrow and shallow, the body
surrounded and covered with leaves and twigs,
the grave filled up with earth and covered
with a mound, into which boughs and veapons
were stuck ; a fire was lighted at the foot, and
circles were cut on adjacent trees (Scott Nind).
Near Eyre’s Sand-patch, i.e., from Point Culver
for 150 miles eastward and for 40 miles inland, the
dead were simply laid on the ground with the
head to the east; a little food was placed near the
right hand, and a small tire lighted near by
(Williams).
* * #
Near Port Lincoln, S.A., in Eyre’s Penin-
sula, the body was buried on its back with the
knees drawn up, covered with kangaroo skin;
stakes were placed across the grave, on which
earth was piled, and a fence of boughs was placed
around (Wilhelmi). In Yorke’s Peninsula, the
dead were shoved into old wombat holes (Fowler).
On the shores of Encounter Bay, children still-
born or killed at birth were burned, but other dead
children were packed up, carried by the mother
by day and used as her pillow at night for months,
then exposed on a tree till the bones were cleaned,
when they were buried. Bodies of grown men
were placed in the sun between two fires till the
loosened skin could be peeled off; after flaying,
the openings of the body were sewn up, the body
greased and red-ochred, and placed, with face to
the east and arms extended on a raised platform
over a slow fire till dry ; the smoked remains were
carried about for mouths. Finally, the trunk and
limbs were left on another raised stage to decay,
but the skull, when cleaned up, was utilised by
the next of kin as a drinking vessel (Meyer).
Sometimes, bodies were placed in a tree to decay
(Davis). Along the coast, from the Murray Lakes
to Lacepede Bay, the Narrinyerri sometimes
burned children, sometimes buried them, but dead
warriors were dealt with as at Encounter Bay
(Taplin). On the east shore of Lake Torrens, the
dying were waddied to death, and the fat and tit-
bits cooked and eaten (Gason). In the country of
the Dieri, bordered by Cooper’s Creek on the
north, and a line from Freeling heights to Depot
Glen on the south, after the body was lowered
into the grave, a non-relative jumped in, cut off
all the fat from the face, thighs, arms, and
stomach, and deceased’s kin ate it; the grave was
surmounted by a pile of wood, food was placed
near by, and fires lighted round about (Gason).
Just north of Depot Glen the corpse was smeared
with gypsum before burial (Webster).
• * *
At Port Darwin (N.T.), among the Larrakia
tribe, warriors were either buried at once, or the
body was tied up in the branches of a tree till the
flesh rotted off and the bones fell to the ground; the
bones were then buried (Foelsche). At Raffles
Bay, on Coburg Peninsula, women and children
were promptly buried without ceremony ; but
dead warriors were wrapped in grass and sus-
pended from a branch till the flesh rotted off and
the bones fell to the ground ; the bones were then
collected, painted red, carried about for months,
and finally buried near deceased’s birthplace
(Foelsche). In some parts of Arnheim’s Land
bodies of warriors were exhumed after being
buried for five days, wrapped in bark and carried
about by the kin (Duncan).
* • «
On the Cloncurry, Q., the Miappe tribe
cooked their dead in a trench lined with heated
stones and filled in with hot embers, and the
adults ate the baked body (Curr). Between the
Cloncurry and the Flinders the dead were some-
times eaten, sometimes buried beneath a mound
covered with logs and boughs (M'Gillivray). On
the Granite Range, a few miles inland from
Cairns, the dead were either eaten or else tied up
in ti-tree bark and buried (Mowbray). Round
Halifax Bay, the body was eaten (Johnstone).
On the head-waters of the Burdekin children were
eaten by their distant kin, women and old men
buried without ceremony, and warriors placed on
stages in trees. In the Townsville district a large
fire was made over the grave (Johnstone). At
Mt. Elliott, just south of Townsville, the dead
were eaten by their kin (Gregory). Round Mary-
borough male corpses were skinned, dried, carried
for mouths, then buried (M'Pherson). Between
Brisbane and Gympie the Mooloola tribe skinned
the corpse, ate the flesh, and placed the bones in
a tree (Westaway). On Great Sandy (Fraser’s)
Island young men and fat young women
were eaten; a dead warrior’s skin an 1 bones
were dried and divided among lus rela-
tives; sometimes bodies were burn <1 and
the ashes carried about (Police D pi.); or
the body was buried in its rugs (Fison); or, if the
corpse was that of a young man, it was skinned,
the flesh being dried in lumps and the marrow ex-
tracted from the bone, thereafter the parts were
carried till stale, then placed on a stage in a tree
(Fison); occasionally the complete body was
placed in a tree (Fison). At the source of the
Mary River, the Kabi tribe ate the flesh, buried
the large bones under a log-pile, and kept the
small bones as relics (Mathew). On Stradbroke
and Moreton Island burial was the rule (Watkin).
Between Hughenden and the head of the Cape
River, the Mungerra tribe buried in shallow
graves (Armstrong). At Natal Downs fat men
accidentally killed were eaten, boys, girls and
women burnt; the internal organs of a warrior
were buried separately from the body proper,
which was exhumed at the end of a month, when
the skull and bones were cleaned, placed on a
stage in a tree, taken down three months later,
and carried about by the female relatives till they
tired of them (Chattield). In the district enclosed
by Natal Downs, Elgin Downs, Bowen Downs
and Tower Hill the accidentally-killed were eaten
and their bones buried ; old women were burned
and their ashes buried ; old men were raised on a
stage five feet high for three days, then wrapped
in ti-tree bark and buried beneath a log-pile ;
when decay was complete, the bones were carried
for a time, then dropped into a hollow tree
(M'Glashan). On the Belyando, a warrior’s corpse
was buried for two months, exhumed, chopped up,
wrapped in bark, carried by female relatives till the
death was avenged, then dropped into a hollow tree;
women were buried off-hand (Muirhead). At Peak
Downs the widow carried her husband’s remains
for months before burying them (Murray). At
the headwaters of the Burke, the flesh was eaten
and the bones buried (Eglinton); also here and
there on the Herbert (Maohattie). On the Dia-
mantina and the Herbert, many tribes dug a grave
with its long axis north and south, placed cane
grass on the bottom, and at sunrise laid the naked
corpse thereon at full length, covered it with grass
and filled up the grave with earth (Curr). At the
source of the Bareoo, males were buried for a time,
exhumed, carried in bark coffins for six months,
then re-buried (Hyde). At the junction of the
Thomson and Bareoo, the dead were buried in
sandhills (Heagney). In places in Cooper’s Greek
district, the young men dug a grave, while the
elders tied the corpse’s big toes together and the
hands behind the back ; the body was laid length-
wise in the grave, which was then filled with dead
wood and surmounted by a wood-pile (James).
Among the tribes between the Maranoa and Paroo,
the body was sometimes burned with its belong-
ings ; sometimes mummified, carried about, and
later placed in a hollow tree ; sometimes the hair
was cut off, the face painted with clay, and the
corjise then buried (Playfair).
* • •
In N.S.W., the Kamilaroi tribe on the
Barwan, Gwydir and Namoi rivers buried their
dead (Bucknell). On the Hunter, the Wonnarua
buried under a pile of logs (Miller). On the Bogan,
the natives had cemeteries resembling those of
Europeans, in which the graves were numerous
and the grounds ornamented, and they had
curved walks or tracks through them. One of
these cemeteries, Milmeridien, is thus described
by Major Mitchell: “This burial-ground was
a fairy-like spot, in the midst of a scrub of droop-
ing acacias. It was an extensive space, laid out
in little walks, which were narrow and smooth,
as if intended only for ‘spirits.’ All these ran in
gracefully curved lines, and enclosed the heaving
heaps of reddish earth, which contrasted finely
with the acacias and dark oasuarinas around.
Others, gilt with moss, shot far into the recesses
of the bush, where slight traces of still more
ancient graves proved the antiquity of these
simple but touching records of humanity. With
all our art we could do no more for the dead than
these poor savages had done.” Charles Reade
poetically describes the same place in chapter 70
of ‘‘lt is Never too Late to Mend.” Between
Bourke and the Paroo the corpse was buried forth-
with, and a little hut, in which was placed a
vessel of water, was built over the grave (Scriv-
ener). Round Bourke the dead were buried in the
sand-hills, above flood-mark, after impressive cere-
monies, blood from the head of the chief mourner
being sprinkled over the leaves in the bottom of
the grave before the body was put in (Teulon). On
the Darling the grave-mounds were covered with
branches, surrounded by a ditch and a fence of
dead limbs and branches (Mitchell). On the
Lachlan the graves were marked by high mounds,
around which were placed rude seats (Mitchell,
Oxley). On the Murrumbidgee and Murray
graves were covered with thatched huts (Mitchell).
At the junction of these two rivers the graves of
males were surrounded by double or triple low
ridges, a hut was erected over them, which was
burned after the death was avenged (Mitchell).
* * *
In Victoria, on the Delatite, Ovens,
Broken and King Rivers, the body was conveyed
to the deceased’s birthplace; if it were that of a
warrior it was burned and the bones placed in a
hollow tree or branch; in the case of a child, a
hollow branch was carefully cleaned out, and a
bed of leaves made, on which the body was laid
in a rude coffin of bark peeled from a sapling, and
the opening carefully plugged up ; married people
were burned (Smyth). The Goulburn blacks sank
a shaft five feet deep and then made a horizontal
drive at bottom about 3ft. long and 2ft. Gin. high ;
the body was placed on leaves in this drive, its
aperture closed with bark, the shaft tilled in with
earth, and a shield-shaped mound piled up at foot
end of grave and at right angles to it (Smyth).
In Central Victoria those who died of a loathsome
disease were burned; young, clean bodies were put
into a tree to decay and the bones afterwards
buried ; warriors, old men and doctors were buried
with much ceremony; in special cases the grave
was covered in with boughs and fenced round
with stakes (Stanbridge). In Southern Vic-
toria there were no cemeteries; a warrior had
his rugs, weapons, Ac., buried with him in
a grave covered with a large mound and fenced in
with boughs; women and children were buried
unceremoniously except that a fire was lit close to
the mound; in the case of a young girl the fire
was on top of the mound (Smyth). The Barra-
bool blacks near Geelong stuck a fighting stick at
the east end of a young man’s grave (Smyth).
* * *
In Tasmania the body was placed upright
in a hollow tree, left for 12 months to decay, and
then burned, except the skull, which was carried
to a sort of savage Calvary, and there left in the
place of skulls (Davies); sometimes the dead were
burned, sometimes buried, sometimes thrown into
a natural hole and covered with leaves and rubbish
(Milligan).

The Three Friends of Uhomotu.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The white trader of Uhomotu came to his
door and looked seawards at the smoky haze
which almost hid the ocean swell sweeping west-
ward from Beveridge Reef, 300 miles away, to
crash against the grey coral cliffs that lined the
weather side of the island from Uhomotu to Liku.
In the village, sweltering under the groves of
cjco inut and breadfruit, not a sign of life was
visible. Your true Polynesian dreads heat as
much as cold.
* # #
“What an infernal day, and what a
horrible-looking coast,” muttered the trader as he
looked at the line of cliffs rising a sheer hundred
feet from the boiling surf at their base. Now
and then a heavy roller would hurl itself against
the wall of rock and leap high in air, drenching
with spray the stunted, tangled scrub that
covered the jagged summits of the cliffs to their
very edge, and pouring down the face in a fierce
white avalanche of roaring foam.
The tide was falling, arid here and there, at the
foot of the wall of rock, the trader could see the
protruding mounds and knobs of the black reef of
coral which, at low tide, formed a border of relief
to the cold grey of the cliffs above it. For 20 feet
or so the reef stood out, and then, like the coast
behind it, presented a perpendicular weed-clad
face to the rolling Pacific.
» * *
The hot, depressing calm irritated the
trader. He was not a drinking man, or he might
have drunk till the land-breeze set in and cooled
the air.
“I’ll shut up the store and camp under the
teacher’s orange-trees ; it’s cool there—hallo, what
do you want ?”
A native boy was standing in the room, holding
out a piece of paper. It was written upon in the
usual sprawling manner of natives, and was a
request to hand the bearer the articles mentioned.
“In the name of the evil spirits, who be these
that write— Maheke, Kitia, and Minea?” he
asked crossly.
“ Mahcke, Kitia ma Minea.”
“O wood-head ! am I any wiser now ?”
The boy stared solemnly, and then by a sudden
inspiration showed him a roll of money tied up in
the end of his waist-cloth.
“Ha !” said the trader, “ now do I see. Stolen
money, eh ? And these women have sent thee to
spend it. Now will I call for the fakajili (judge)
and have thee beaten.”
“ Nay, nay,” whined the boy, “ I be honest.”
“ Then why come to this door, which is tabu.
Do I buy or sell in this room ? Have I not a fate
koloa (store) ? ”
“ True, but I was forbidden to go there lest I
be seen.”
“Ha, ’tis stolen money then, else why fear to
be seen ?”
“ Mahekfi forbade me.”
“ And who is Maheke ? ”
“ The friend of Kitia.”
“ And who is Kitia ?”
“The friend of Minea.”
“O dolt, O half-awakened hog. How do I
know these names! Who, in God’s name, is
Minea ? ”
“ She be friend to Kitia and MahekS—they bo
friends to one another.”
“So. I see. These three then have stolen the
money between them— the fakafili ”
The boy began to blubber —“Nay, it is not so.
It is honest money. And these three gave it me
with the tuhi (letter) for thee, and bade me tell no
one. And if I came safely to them I was to have
one piece of money for myself, which Mahekfi
hath now in her hand.”
* * *
The trader was puzzled. It was so unusual
for native women to send anyone to spend money
for them. The rule was to make their purchases
ostentatiously, and show everyone that they had
money. He read the note again.
“Three white handkerchiefs, three white poll
combs, a bottle of musk, three pili dlo (chemises)
blue gossamer, tobacco and matches.”
* # #
“ What the deuce can it be ! ” he thought,
as he went into the adjoining store. He got the
articles named, and tied them into a parcel. Then
he looked again at the rude scrawling signatures :
“Tor us, Maheke, Minea, ma Kitia.’’
“ Here, boy, take these. What is thy name ? ”
“ Vetsi.”
“ So. And who are these women that send thee
to buy ? Hast thou three wives ? Who is
MahekS ? ”
The boy laughed at the pleasantry, and com-
menced : “ Maheke is the friend
The trader darted out his hand, caught him by
the shoulder, and shook him. “Now, tell me
where does Mahekd live?”
“In XJhomotu, with her mother. She it is
whose lover died in the Pokula (guano islands)
last year.”
“Good. And Minea?”
The parrot-like repetition of “ She is ” was
again commencing, when another shake brought
the boy to his senses.
“Minea is the thin girl with the foot that
wasteth away.”
“ Ha ! ” said the trader, and he asked no further
questions, while the boy, glad to be released, went
cautiously away with the parcel.
* * #
Although not yet six months on the
islands, and unfamiliar with the names of many
people in his own locality, the trader now remem-
bered these three girls. Sometimes they would
bring fruit or a little cotton for sale, and, unlike
the generality of the people, who would hang
about and bandy words with him, they would
take payment in cash and quietly go away.
Oueofthem, Minea, walked with a stick. She
was the youngest of the three, and her two com-
panions seemed tenderly anxious for her. Some
terrible bone-disease had crippled her foot, which
was slowly wasting away. Maheke), a sombre-
faced, black-browed creature, had one day been
pointed out to the trader as the girl who refused
to marry a man of her parents’ choice, for which
contumacy she received many thrashings. Of
Kitia he knew nothing, except that she was the
pretty and inseparable companion of the other
two.
# • •
“ Ani, said he to the teacher’s daughter,
a fat, sullen-faced girl, “ who be the three girls,
Kitia, Minea and Maheke?”
The sullen features lightened up vindictively.
Ah ! they were a bad, lazy lot. Maheke! the
shameless creature that would not marry a good
man like Paturei, who was a deacon. And why ?
Because she had a dead lover in Pokula. She
wanted more beatings. Kitia, an idle little beast,
that the white men favored because she dressed
her head with flowers and sang lewd Samoan
songs, and walked with bare bosom to the bathing-
place, which th a fakafili had forbidden, because it
was not modest. Minea, she who was once so
saucy and was now smitten by God for her sins—
“Oh, shut up, you putty-faced devil! ” said the
trader disgustedly, in English.
1 hou art but as a stranger here,” the girl com-
menced again oilily, “and these are girls whose
names have been called aloud at the fono for their
bad ways. They are three friends—a fourth there
is ; that is the Devil.”
“ Ah !” said the white man, mockingly, “then
have they a strong friend. Perhaps ’tis he that
giveth them so much money to spend in my idle
koloa. ’’
“ What money ?” said she, quickly.
The trader, for amusement, magnified the pur-
chases of the Three Friends. The teacher’s
daughter he knew tobea greedy, maliciouscreature,
and it pleased him to torment her.
Suddenly, with a cry of alarm, the fat Ani tore
past the astonished trader into the village, calling
out something about the Three Friends and the
cliffs of Uhomotu.
Before the white man could get to the village
every soul had left it, their brown bodies dashing
aside the shrubs and cotton bushes that lay in
their way as they hastened, bawling and yelling,
to the cliffs. Wondering if they had all gone mad,
he followed.
• # •
At a point called Matasuafa, where the
perpendicular face of the cliffs was steepest, the
natives—men, women and children—clustered
like bees. Those in front, holding with one hand
the branches of the tough scrub that grew on the
summit, gazed down at the black ledge of reef
that stood abruptly out from the foot of the cliff.
There, directly beneath, lay three figures in female
dress. Descent at this spot was impossible, and
the eyes of the watchers on top moved alternately
from the huddled-up forms beneath to the figures
of four or five men who were running along the
narrow table of reef a few hundred yards away.
The tide was dead low ; yet as the half-naked
men sprangacross the pools and air-holes that broke
up the crust of the reef, the ocean swell broke
savagely against its face and smothered them in
misty spray. And now and again a roller heavier
than the rest would send a flying sheet of water
hissing along the ledge of rock to sway to and fro
the limp hands and garments of the Three Friends.
It came up clear as crystal; it poured back again
through the gutters and air-holes to the sea tinged
with a bloody stain.
* * *
The men dashed on and picked them up,
and then fought their way back along the ledge of
cruel, black rock to a place where a narrow path
had been cut away in a break of the cliffs.
For some time the trader tried to get near them
to see if by any chance they yet lived. Whilst
waiting on the cliffs he had learnt the meaning of
the mysterious purchase of the morning. After
the boy Vetsi in a lonely sugar-cane
patch, the girls had dressed themselves in their
best, carefully oiling and combing their long,
glossy hair. Then, after making and smoking
some cigarettes and sprinkling one another with
scant, they bade the boy come with them. They
travelled an old, unused path of former days,
unknown to the boy, who now began to get
frightened, and cried.
• • •
The girls stopped, and Mahekd, taking
the boy’s hand, placed in it the half-dollar she
had promised him. She bade him go back, but
the lame girl, Minea, who seemed moved some-
what, took the boy to her bosom and kissed and
fondled him. Then she pushed him away, and
with the other two supporting her weakly frame,
they struck into the undergrowth that quivered to
the shock of the breakers dashing against the
face of Matasuafa.
• * *
The trader pushed silently through the
people and looked. Two, MahekS and Minea,
were dead. Their agony had been brief. The
third, the round-faced, laughing-voiced Kitia,
who was but budding into womanhood, still lived,
but that it was not for long could easily be seen.
Both legs and her back were broken.
A woman, with shaking hands and streaming
face, bent over her and spoke.
The girl’s eyes opened, big, soft, black and
tender.
“ EM, where art thou, MahekS . . . and
thou, my Minea. . . Shall I fail thee, Omy
friends . . . my friends.”
The woman laid her lips to the dying mouth
“My child, my Kitia, ’tis I, thy old mother ! ”
The bruised and bleeding fingers twitched
feebly, and then Ani, the Bitter-Tongued, knelt
and, raising the girl’s arms, placed the hands
against her mother’s cheek, and kept them there.
The woman sobbed a question, and, in a faint
whisper, the answer came.
“We had sworn it . . . long, long ago. ’Twas
when MahekS’s lover died we planned it. ‘ I will
die ere I am wife to Paturei,’she said. . . . We
were friends . . . friends. And Minea said
ihen 1 die with thee; for I suffer pain always—
always. And then I, I who was strong and well,
I jumped, too, for we were friends, and I had
sworn to them . . . to be with them . . .
always . . . forever. My mother . . . so old
art thou. . , .
* * #
The trader, with a sudden mist dimming
his eyes, and holding his hat in his hand, stood
back, and turned his face to the sea. Then he
walked slowly home to the village.
As he emerged from the narrow path into the
open the chill of the dewy night-breeze struck
upon his face.
He stopped a moment on the hill, listening,
bwas but the muffled boom of the rollers on
Matasuafa, sounding in loud, long symphony the
requiem of the Three Friends of Uhomotu.
